A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
.£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash value of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these policies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments—more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education in the leading colleges
and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIYATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may bo eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature ago oladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during

SATURDAY, MARCH 26, 1892.
The Savage with a Weak Constitution.
The proposed revival of the Kanaka traffic iu
Queensland concents all Australia. Federation
may come or may stop away, but the Austral!as
have got to live together in peace or share
the fate of the Kilkenny cats. The plan-
tation aristocracy of the Southern States
came into collision with the while democracy
of the North and the result was the bloodiest
civil war of modern days; and for the same
causes, should the capitalistic party in the North
succeed in making Queensland a plantation-
state, with servile labour for its corner-stone,
alas for the cause of Australian union ! What
Queensland would become under planter rule
may be surmised from the changed tone
apparent in the capital since the black labour
revival. Political morality has gone down as
the temperature falls on the near approach of
an iceberg; and the capitalistic press hardly
think it worth while to clothe with sophistical
reasonings their bald declaration that Govern-
ment and Parliament are entitled to do any-
thing that they can by force effect. It would
seem absurd and useless for a Government to
seek to reverse the policy of a country, just
before a general election, but there is a deep
reason for the course proposed to be taken in
Queensland. The new black-labour party in
their hearts do not believe that the opinion
of the country is changed. If they did
they would have the general election first
and the change of policy after. But they
know they have a condemned, played-out
Parliament to deal with, and they believe that
under threat of a penal dissolution the Assembly
will pass a black-labour law, although live-
sixths of its members are pledged against it.
Once passed, they trust that the wail about
“ capital invested on the faith of Parliament ”
will restrain the incoming Legislature from
reversing their action, or, should that fail, they
think that the Council can be relied upon as
usual to thwart the will of the people.
# # *
The political situation in Queensland at this
moment would be impossible in almost any
other country in the world, and it would not be
believed of Queensland if the proofs were not so
absolutely complete and unanswerable. A
Government, the leading members of which are
pledged up to their eyes to oppose the rcintro-
duction of coloured labour, declare through the
press their intention of forcing a measure for
its reintroduetion through a Representative
Chamber five-sixths of the members of which
are also solemnly pledged to oppose it. This,
too, at the end of t he fourth year of a Parlia-
ment that lias passed a Triennial Parliaments
Bill ! And honest, reputable men calmly
assume that there will be traitors enough and
to spare to ensure its passage. Consistency is
openly scoffed at, and the man who will not go
back on his pledges is made the target of Inc
dominant faction and their servile press. One
Minister of the Crown said in Parliament on
23rd October, 1890
The force in favour of hlack-labour is n coherent, active
force; it is o wealthy body having paid emissaries in
every part of the colony ; whereas the people opposed to
t are not coherent, their occupation as a rule requiring
them to wander about the country to a large extent. The
miners would be the great opponents of black-labour.
The person who uttered these words in 1800
is the Minister of Mines in the present Govern-
ment, one of the representatives of a great
mining constituency—The Burke —and he is
now using all his influence and his great
abilities to force black labour on the country
whilst the hands of his constituents are tied
through their mistake in accepting him as their
representative. The work of this wealthy,
ever active body is now abundantly apparent
though it is working through new instruments.
The manufacture of a spurious public opinion
designed to furnish weak or unprincipled
members of Parliament with an excuse for
violating their election pledges is its first aim.
And unfortunately the press is to a great extent
at its command. Every little tin-pot associ-
ation, consisting of three men and a paid secre-
tary, by passing a resolution applauding the
manly and straightforward action of the
Premier in deserting his party and turning his
back ou his whole political life, can have its
name and fame transmitted by wire (probably
the first intimation of its existence) to the far
ends of Australia ; but if a meeting of electors
properly called together exhibits any lingering
respect for good faith and honesty, its proceed-
ings are either suppressed or so vilely distorted
that no mortal can find out the truth from the
report. The bogus-petition trick is being
worked for all it is worth. Petitions are got
up for presentation to Parliament, signed by
working-men—not, however, by the unfortunate
wretches out of employment to whom the 15s.
a week and rations of the “free” Kanaka
would be a godsend, but by men in full employ-
ment who, for fear of losing their billets, are
compelled to bow themselves down in the house
of Rimmon and pretend to worship with their
employer at the altar of servile labour.
* * *
But chiefly this wealthy body—or rather the
brainy men in its employment —have been busy
lately in making little perches for “ converts ”
to hop ou to in their passage across from
Liberalism to the dominant faction. They are
poor, rotten twigs at best, but the flimsiest
support is welcome to the feet of a convert
with a little conscience left. The three most
highly-favoured by the tender-feet, and, there-
fore, most assiduously recommended, arc :
1. Tropical agriculture requires tlie labour specially
fitted by nature for it (/.«., of course, the Kanaka).
2. Iu future there will be no abuses in connection with
recruiting.
3. In future the Kanaka will not come into competition
with the white man.
The complete answer to the first argument is
—the Mortality Table. The white man can do
the work, but doesn’t like it; the Kanaka
likes it as little, but can’t do it so well. If the
white man gets sick and tired of it the
Kanaka gets sick and tired of it. And it is
that particular work that kills him. If he
died on his native islands at half the rate ho
dies in growing cane iu Queensland, he would
have become extinct long before now. On one
plantation, out of 78 Kanakas, 23 died in ten
months. Will white men and women who go
to church ou Sunday and subscribe during the
week to the Society for the Prevention of
Cruelty to Animals pause and think of that!
Two doctors, sent up by the Queensland
Government to investigate matters reported : —•
Tht Kanaka plantation in Maryborough district might
also be looked upon ns an adult male one, and yet in
the year 1879 the mortality was 74 per 1000, while on the
Yengarie, Yarra Yarrn, and Irrawana, the plantations
belonging to K. Crax and Co., the mortality in the flve-
nnd-a-quarter years ending 31st March, 1880, was 92 per
1000, and for the year 1879, 107 per 1000, and for the
three months ending 31st March, 1880, 100 per 1000.
« * »
This shows how ungratefully the Kanaka
behaves towards his employers when engaged
in the work for which he is specially fitted.
When they get away to other occupations they
don’t die quite so fast ; hut taken all round in
Queensland they die about four times as easily
as the whites ! Taking the census period—from
1886 to 1891 —there have been in the province
about 10,000 Kanakas, 8000 of whom are sup-
posed to be engaged iu growing sugar. The
officially-recorded deaths are ; 1886, 598 ; 1887,
513; 1888, 524; 1889, 492; JB9O, 450 ; total
funeral bill for five years, 2577. The death-
rate of Europeans iu Queensland in 1890 was
12.81 per 1000 ; of Kanakas during the same
year, 46.44. The Registrar-General of Queens-
land in his latest report has something interest-
ing to say about the Kanaka death-rate. He
says:—-
This noticeable high death-rate of Polynesians may be
accounted for in several ways, some of which causes, no
doubt, lead to consumption, “ the disease most prevalent
amongst people of this race.” The inbreeding for long
periods of certain tribes on the Islands produces a race
which seems peculiarly liable to contract phthisis, so that
the change of climate from the humid atmosphere pre-
vailing in the islands of the South Seas to the drier air of
this continent, and the great difference in diet, &c.,n1l
tend (o predispose a naturally weak constitution to a
disease most fatal amongst (hem.
• • •
A naturally weak con dilution. This is the
great secret. From this source springs that
quality which has endeared the Kanaka to the
plantation-owner his docility. When he
doesn’t die altogether, he remains half-dead
and quite submissive. He is priceless to the
planter, because pre-eminently kickable. In
Queensland, at the present time, the Almighty
is freely accused of having expressly designed
him to thrash canc for the MTlwraithian
capitalist. Ho is the rock upon which the
future greatness of Queensland is going to be
built up. I'lie great combination Government
lias achieved a triumph of statesmanship. A
community of nearly 400,000 whites, with a
country abounding in natural resources, is going
to be saved from ruin by the enforced labours
of some 8000 savages with weak constitutions !
Would such a brilliant idea have occurred to a
common Labour member not versed ir the
science of Government ?
• • •
The second perch is soon knocked over. One
of the reasons that actuated Parliament in
, trying to stop the labour-recruiting, was the
conviction forced upon it by facts that tire
abuses were inseparable from the traffic. When
men charged with murder, piracy, and other
South Sea recreations, were tried and con-
victed, what was the excuse put forward for
them by their friends? If, was that those were
incidents of the traffic, and that it was not fair
to visit upon the heads of individuals the sins
of the system under which they worked.
Government and Parliament then confessed
themselves impotent to cleanse the traffic. Are
they more powerful or more moral now ? Is
the morality of the Government at the present
time any higher than that of an African
slaver? Are”they not now contemplating a
gigantic breach of faith with the people of
the province, in forcing this hideous thing again
on a free white community, and can they be
trusted to make even a serious effort to perform
the impossible feat of making a traffic in servile
labour respectable or humane? The black-
labour men declare beforehand they will not
have it—they demand that the traffic shall be
freed from “ those irritating Government inter-
ferences ” and that the planters shall have a
“free hand.” In future, we are told, black
labour is not to come into competition with
white labour. Why in the future more than in
the present ? The law says now that it shall be
restricted to field labour in agricultural produc-
tion, but though for the last eight years at
least the anti-Kanaka party has been in the
ascendant the law has been openly, shamelessly,
and continually violated with the full know-
ledge of every Government that has held office.
Will the pro-Kanaka men, if they get the
upper hand, enforce that law ? They claim now
a “free hand.” The irritating Government
interferences annoy them. If future Kanaka
labour is not to come into competition with
white, why not make a start by compelling the
plantation owners to employ the two or three
thousand Kanakas who are now—in most cases
illegally—competing with white labour ? No,
they will not have these. The “ free ” Kanakas
want wages; they want to be treated like
men ; they are not kickable. The black-labour
men will never respect a law that cuts against
them ; an anti-Kanaka Government could never
make them, and a pro-Kanaka Government
will never try to. The talk about preventing
Kanakas from coming into competition with
white men is pure bosh and treachery ;
it is dust raised by the special pleaders
of the black-labour party to throw into
the eyes of the white workers. The big
plantation-owners want to keep stocking
their plantations with continual relays of
newly-caught savages. If allowed to do that,
what is the inevitable result ? The fat,
seasoned, so-called “free” Kanaka, who has
acquired the white man’s vices and lost his old
facility for dying, will over-slop into the towns
in constantly increasing numbers, and be an
ever-darkeuing curse on the community —a
curse- which, alas ! too often falls, not upon
wealth, whose greed has evoked it, but upon
helpless want that lives outside the shields and
safeguards behind which the luxurious rich lie
securely entrenched. Every year swells the
ranks of the “free” Kanaka, who will not go
away or die, but will stop and breed.
# # *
The partizans supporting the Government in
their new departure are also cogitating another
brilliant idea. Bui ikjet, the domestic servant,
is not sufficiently reliable. They think a nice
female Kanaka could do all the work possibly
for less wages and without “ talking back.” So
a deputation of planters and farmers at Buuda-
berg have asked the Minister for Lands—who
la the one consistent, unchanging supporter of
black slave-labour in the Cabinet—that the
female islanders who came to Queensland might
be allowed to “ assist in housework.” Even
Mr. Cowley had to admit himself rather aston-
ished at this new development, and he is going
to consult the “illustrious convert,” Sir Sam.
Griffith, before committing himself. What
Queensland is now wo know ; what it will be
in ten years’ time we can only surmise from the
policy that is, adopted. Let us look at the census
returns: —The total populationou Ist May, 1886,
was 322,853. On sth April, 1891, it was 393,718.
In five years the population was increased by
70,865. Free and assisted immigration during
that period accounts for over 40,(MX) of that
number. The balance is not equal to the
natural increase. The population is not actually
dwindling, but it is approaching the stationary
point. Let white Australia pause and think
over this ;—The Coalition Government propose
to revive the Kanaka labour traffic ; and they
are now listening to the prayer of their sup-
porters that the female Kanaka shall be per-
mitted to oust the white girl from, the house-
hold. If this party succeed iu their designs,
what will Queensland be ten years hence ? The
whole middle class—small traders, artisans,
white servants —will be crushed out or reduced
to the condition of mean whites, and Queens-
land, glorious Queensland! teeming with wealth,
agricultural and mineral—will bccomea paradise
of the Devil, inhabited by two classes, the
MTlwraithian capitalist and the savage with a
weak constitution.
Inspectors of Agriculture.
About the last official act of the Parkes
Government in New South Wales was the
establishment of a Department of Agriculture,
and about the first official act of the Dibbs
Government was the appointment of four
inspectors of agriculture in connection there-
with, to pervade the country and encourage the
bucolic industry generally. The duties of an
inspector of agriculture are not very well
defined, but presumably he will travel round
and look with a vacant eye at oats and other
vegetables, and will gaze listlessly at potatoes,
and pass on, and draw Ids salary. The oats
will grow no better for being inspected, and
(lie potatoes will not improve in any way
under the light of the official eye, and, so far as
we arc aware, no one anticipates that they will.
Hut when any Australian industry is languish-
ing it is always regarded as the correct tiling to
appoint some pompous official to inspect it, and
some day, on the same principle, it will he
found advisable to inspect the Australian him-
self when he is languishing, and somebody will
he appointed at a Targe salary to supervise old
men, and to look at persons with weak spines
and tottery logs. For inspection, to the official
mind, is a species of fertiliser which makes
things grow with renewed vigour, and when the
building trade falls flat some future Go vein-
meat will endeavour to galvanise it into new
life by appointing an inspector of dead walls,
and when the floods prove unusually disastrous
in some year of rain and misfortune a Depart-
ment of Slush will be created to fix its eye on
the debris left behind by the overflowing rivers
of a sodden continent. Yet, strangely enough,
the same men whose faith in the inspection of
other people is so boundless and sublime have
no confidence in the nostrum when applied to
themselves. It was Pauk.es who first con-
ceived the idea of appointing itinerant officers
to go round and supervise the agricultural
industry, and the rage of Parkes when any-
body supervises him is something to be
remembered.
* * *
The agricultural industry of New South
Wales is a failure, for various reasons. The
first essential of the agriculturist is a market
for his produce, and as half the population of
the province is crowded into the city of
Sydney, that capital is practically the only
market available. But under the Freetrade
regime, with low sea-freights and enormously
expensive land-carriage thrown in, this has been
practically closed to the great mass of New South
Wales farmers. The cheap grain of the pro-
tected agriculturists of California and Maori-
land has supplied most of the city’s mpiire-
ments, and the wheat-grower of the far interior
left his unsaleable crops to rot for want of a
purchaser. The lack of water conservation, too,
makes the prospects of the selector exceedingly
precarious. His work is hard ; and dull, heavy,
monotonous, unremunerative toil under the
burning summer sun of Australia has few
attractions. There is little joy in digging pain-
fully and slowly upon the great immensity of a
waterless plain, where no green thing is visible
from one horizon to the other, and where the
labour seems as hopeless as the effort to grow
flowers upon a gallows. The method sof New South
Wales agriculture are too horribly and mono-
tonously English. There is no necessity for
elaborate irrigation in England, therefore the
Cornstalk farmer docs not irrigate, and the rest
of his system, or want of system, is on the same
pattern. Here and there, in the howling wastes
of the alleged arable lauds, some Frenchman or
German, with that ready adaptability which
distinguishes his race from the cast-iron Anglo-
Saxon, has constructed a paradise by suiting
himself to local conditions, but these spots are
few and far between. What the Australian
farmer wants, in the first instance, is to forget
that there is, or ever was, such a place as
England, to abolish English agricultural pro-
fessors, and to make a bonfire of all the English
works on agriculture, and to start afresh on
Chinese or Indian or Algerian principles.
* # *
Moreover, the young Australian hates the
bush with a great and holy hatred, and he
loathes the cart-horse and the plough and all
the other concomitants of agriculture. There
is little poetry in his composition ; for that
matter, there is hardly any poetry in any-
body’s composition. The agriculturist, almost
all the world over, is heavy, dull, and
stupid ; the poetic shepherd of fiction and
drama is a lie, and the joyous peasant
gill of romance is just as impossible and
as incongruous as a romantic executioner
or a genile, unsophisticated soul in the gaol-
flogging business. The bush Ihpior is bad, and
the bush public-house is remote and unattrac-
tive ; there is no theatre among the wilds, no
nigger-minstrel show, no boxing saloon, no tug-
of-war, no evening paper with the tale of an
“ ’Orrid Deed ” told in huge, black capitals, no
waxworks, nothing. Even the dreary Stanley
does not go there to tell, in the dead level of
his solemn monotone, the tale of how he found
Livingstone. And instead of all these joys
there are great, glaring wastes of earth, a burn-
ing sun ; the dull, uneventful snake and the
occasional lizard ; sand-flies, sand, and sandy-
blight; the skeleton and rusty billy and rusty,
iron-shod boots, now and then, of some lost
swagsman ; a kangaroo at long intervals; a
nigger here and there ; a long, long vista of
melancholy, barbed-wire fences spreading all
through eternity—and that is about all. Even
the alleged music of the river on its bars of
golden sand is a failure, for the river, in most
cases, is a chain of stagnant water-holes with
the heads of dead cattle emerging from the
slime, and when the waterholes become dry,
then the river is a river of ashes flowing
through a land of fire. Amid these surround-
ings t he vegetable pursuits of New South Wales
languish, and even 40,000 inspectors would
make little difference in the situation of affairs.
# # #
The four inspectors of agriculture who are
supposed to revolutionise this blighted industry
are endowed with no particular authority of
any description ; there is nothing definite to do,
and they have no definite power to do it. They
are not even starting to make bricks without
straw ; they are rather starting without straw
to make nothing. When one of them strikes a
selection out on some arid waste in the far west
he is, apparently, authorised in a general way
to lean over the fence and say, in effect, “ How s
things?”—and the selector will probably reply,
in effect, “Go to —.” The inspectors, so fat * 8
can be learned, are merely four dreadfully
ordinary individuals with a dubious smattering
of knowledge on the subject of vegetation.
When there is anything radically wrong m 1 1
methods of agriculture they may be com-
petent to recognise it, or, again, they may
not—-the latter is much the more probable
hypothesis. If they do recognise it and mention
the fact the agriculturist may start a dog a
them, or he may not—that is also a matter ox
conjecture. They have no legal authority
force the misguided, conservative cockatoo o
of his old unprofitable groove and info a n
one ; if they had they would probably or(
the wretched farmer to dig irrigation chann
uphill, or to sow' the seed of the cow r m
autumn so that the animal might come *
before the excessive heats of summer. A
w ill fill up great, elaborate returns with ig l
and statistics and recommendations; 110} .
will ever read the returns, and nobody ' v(l
pay any attention to them if lie did read m ‘
An inspector, even in his highest and no )
form, is only a man who gazes at some n h-
Some inspectors gaze at tilings which j


understand, and some at things which they
don’t understand. Others, again, don’t gaze
a t anything, and they all seem to produce
nothing. #
There are various methods by which the New
South Wales farmer may be raised from his old,
hopeless situation. The establishment of a
protective tariff will give him a tolerably sure
market, in the first place—an advantage which
he has never yet possessed. Next, he urgently
wants a good system of local government, such
as already exists in Queensland and Victoria, so
that he may learn to rely more upon his own
exertions, and less upon the great race of log-
rollers, politicians, department-trotters, and
other frauds. Thirdly, he wants a system of
irrigation and water conservation, conducted
hy focal trusts ; even the best irrigation scheme
would fail if worked entirely by one central
authority, for then the department would pre-
sently degenerate into a centre of corruption,
and water would be sold for votes, and reser-
voirs swapped for political influence, and the
locality which voted straight would have six
artesian wells, and the one that didn’t vote
straight wouldn’t even have a 400-gallon iron
tank. Further, a certain amount of good may
result from the establishment of colleges, where
agriculture would be taught upon a thoroughly
'scientific basis, though the results achieved by
these institutions are, as a rule, woefully dis-
appointing. For, in his grovelling reverence
for everything English, the Australian generally
imports his professors, and these individuals
teach according to the rules and methods of
England, and solemnly warn the students of
this drought-cursed land to avoid excessive
moisture, and instruct the inhabitants of a
country where the summer air is molten fire to
beware of frosts, and to keep an eye upon snow
and intense cold. But even at its worst the
agricultural college has some redeeming features,
and the high-priced inspector has none. For
he only walks around and looks at things as
they are, and things get no better for being
looked at. Moreover, if agriculture is likely
to get better by being inspected, then trade—
which is likewise bad—ought also to be in-
spected to see if it will improve under the same
process; and the Sydney Daily Telegraph
leaders—which are worse—should be inspected
in a similar fashion, and for a similar purpose.
Further, the inspectors themselves may possibly
want to be inspected, and also the officials who
inspect the inspectors, and the process is nearly
interminable.
The Criminal Conscience.
The attempt made by the press and public to
arraign the Windsor and Rainhill murderer on
the ground of ordinary human conscience, and
to pass judgment upon his misdeeds from the
standpoint of the normal man strikes The
Bulletin as simply ludicrous. The man is
essentially an instinctive criminal, and being an
instinctive criminal, he lies entirely outside the
moral range of average humanity. His crimes
may be revolting in the extreme, and the
motives that urged him to commit them utterly
incomprehensible, but it is just about as logical
to descant on the wickedness of the shark or
the ethical callousness of the tiger as to treat the
born murderer as other than an irresponsible
throw-back whose mental and moral develop-
ment has not yet reached the high-water mark
of the modern standard,
• # #
This view does not of necessity compel the
man who holds it to an admission of the injus-
tice of destroying such an enemy to human
solidarity as Williams must be. It only
places the reason for his destruction upon other
and more rational grounds. It denies the puni-
tive effect upon the man himself, and the
deterrent effect upon instinctive criminals
atavistically fashioned in the same mould. It
is simply impossible to punish Williams,
and the long catalogue of his alleged crimes
if accepted as authentic sufficiently demon-
strates that all the hangings carried out
in the name and by the sanction of British
law were not enough to deter him from the
commission of those very crimes for which
others were hanged. If it be necessary
to annihilate Williams, it must be be-
cause he threatens the existence of any
such thing as human society. He is a
revival in our own century of the savage, the
conscienceless and non-ethical man of some
remote age far arear. He may be dangerous,
and therefore a fit subject for destruction, but
he is absolutely irresponsible ; and to confound
him with those whose crimes were consequent
°n passionate outbreaks, which are frequently
the result of too much rather than too little
morality, is to give the denial to all the know-
ledge of criminal psychology which modern
science has patiently and laboriously accumu-
lated. “ The vilest of the red-handed host,”
as a Sydney daily somewhat grandiosely puts
it, have not as a rule very much conscience
"herewith to be “horrified,” and such con-
science as they possess seldom gives much
hy way of ethical results upon analysis.
Ibe \\ illiams of the daily papers is a
m au apart from men. The psychologic
pendulum oscillates but over a very limited
segment of the semicircle of moral or immoral
possibilities in the average mortal. A degree
°r two more in the range of its swing is often
a H the difference between eccentricity and mad*
jiess, crime, and genius, and whether the case
)e one for the doctor or the executioner, the
'"ad-house or the Elmira Reformatory, future
legislators will be compelled to decide, but we
a ie justified, at this late day, in objecting to
ie stupidity that confounds health with
disease, and Othello with Burkk and Hare.
* • •
bi his fascinating volume, “ Erewhon,”
1 amuel Butler devotes a chapter to the
criminal anthropology and psychology of his
utional Utopia, in which he details the differ-
-WS® treatment accorded to the physically
i ro 'n that meted out to the morally
isane, and it is a moot point whether
j'l*. I, * eaa on the subject are not far more
ln °rc conducive to the genuine
\vi • i the human race, than those
iich arc crystallized in the legislation of our
"H tune. In “ Erewhon ” the sick are not
carefully nursed in hospitals ; they are segre-
gated in gaols, or rather lazar-houses, and every
Chance is given them for a reasonable period to
repent the error of their ways. Should they,
however, persist in the crime of sickness, scant
mercy is shown them. They are hustled out of
existence with severe reprehension, lest their
abominable conduct should infect the healthy
outside community. With the ethical offender,
far other means are adopted to win him back to
moral sanity. He is condoled with by his
friends, and carefully tended by doctors and
nurses, until a cure is effected, and he is once
more fit to resume his place in the society of
his fellows.
* * *
We are many thousands of years from
philosophical “ Erewhon,” and we shall go on
lecturing our criminals and hurling press in-
vectives at their heads ; Judges will moralise
when passing sentence upon the enormity of
crimes which the perpetrators never once
reflected upon, or considered in the light of
crimes at all; clergymen will contrive to snatch
the conscienceless souls of criminals from the
burning, and translate to Heaven non-moral
atavistic creatures whose presence would be an
affront to the decent sinners in Gehenna itself ;
and hangmen will continue to “ deter ” the
ethical outcast whose instinctive propensity to
crime is to him what the poison-fang is to the
cobra di capello—and all for what ? Simply
that we may delude ourselves with the idle
fancy that blood can be atoned for by blood,
and that death can be made more fearsome by
much company.
# # *
Let us be logical in this as in other matters.
We kill a noxious reptile simply because it is
noxious. We waste no sympathy and no senti-
ment, one way or the other, over the scorpion
which we crush beneath the heel of our boot.
We do not call a shark names, or urge him to
repentance, before we drive a harpoon into his
carcase. If a man is mad we confine him in an
asylum, but we do not insult him for losing his
reason. So let it be with those whom society
determines to kill for its own safety. Only
honesty and not cant is demanded. If Williams
is so constituted that other men are unable to
live with him without danger to their lives,
then, in the name of logic, face the issue in all
its integrity ; but do not let us hurl childish
fury at a man who is as much an irresponsible
production of natural forces as Mount Vesuvius,
whose crater can no more be extinguished by
pouring down it buckets of water than crime
can be suppressed by the indulgence of rhetorical
rhodomontade.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Weakness of Law.
The case of E. S. Smithurst, a Muclgee
(N.S.W.) journalist, who recently went bank-
rupt under a strange combination of ill-fortune,
is an eloquent example of the weakness of the
civil law of Australia. Mr. Smithurst, some
time ago, bought the Mudgee Independent
from Messrs. Richards and Cohen for £2600,
the sellers being bound by an elaborate, cast-
iron, legal document not to carry on or engage
in any newspaper business, either directly or in-
directly, within 60 miles of Mudgee for a period
of three years, under a penalty of £lOOO.
Shortly after this agreement was entered into
the sellers, according to Smithurst’s evidence,
blandly requested that the buyer should give
them, gratis, a share in the paper which he had
already bought and paid for, failing which they
promised to start a rival journal in defiance of
the bond. Their request being refused, the
new paper was started ; it bore the imprint of
one Charles Knight as the proprietor, but the
evidence of the connection of Cohen and Co.
with the venture was so conclusive that the
Equity Court promptly granted an interim
injunction against them. Of this no notice
whatsoever was taken ; the attitude of
the two defendants amounted practically
to a declaration that the Equity Court
didn’t matter at all. Some few days later
Equity Judse Owen granted a second injunction
endorsed with a notice that the defendants ren-
dered themselves liable to be sent to gaol if
they disregarded it. This second legal docu-
ment also excited in their minds just as much
awe as a piece of brown wrapping-paper; the
two individuals aimed at went placidly on their
way and the Equity Court took the affront in a
Christian spirit. 'Smithurst presently drew
the Law’s attention to the manner in which its
mandate was being disregarded, and, instead of
enforcing its two previous injunctions, it gently
told him to apply for a third one. Injunctions
are expensive, but plaintiff, who was already
nearly insolvent through applying for useless
legal documents, obtained at considerable ex-
pense a third piece of paper to the same effect
as the other two. Along with it a warrant was
issued for the defendants’ arrest in case they
disobeyed, but as the document was not allowed
to be put in force unless they “ repeated their
offence ” it was just as useful as if the Court
had issued a bow and arrow, or a piccp of green
cheese, or any other extraneous substance. The
defendants only smiled a peaceful smile at the
third injunction ; and when the plaintiff re-
quested that the warrant should be executed
against them, it was suggested in a perfectly
friendly manner that perhaps he had better
proceed in the customary manner and obtain
some more injunction. He would probably
have done so—-in fact he might have got several
more injunctions—but just at this stage his
funds ran out, and he became insolvent. At
latest dates the two defendants, despite three
injunctions and a warrant, were still prosper-
ing. The man to whom the Equity Court gave
its unqualified support is bankrupt and penni-
less, and the individuals whom the Court
started out to suppress arc booming along
cheerfully. Possibly, if Smithurst had applied,
in the name of Cohen and Richards, for a
legal prohibition against himself, it might have
been a more successful experiment; that is to
say he might have prospered, while Cohen and
Co. went bung instead. An injunction and an
old Waterloo mnsket appear to be very much
alike ; it is safer to stand in front of either of
them, than behind.
Breeclies-Pocket Christianity.
Rev. Dr. White, Moderator of the New South
Wales Presbyterian General Assembly, is a
well-fed, thoroughly satisfied, able-bodied
Christian who has evidently forgotten all that
he ever read about Christ. His theories, as
reported, are squared to support the rich pew-
renters and the mammon-loaded lights of the
Kirk who while away their week-days finessing
with the world and make long prayers on Saw-
baths. Says Pastor White :
Man brings nothing into the world with him but th e
obligation to obey, without question and without hesita'
tion.
Obey whom? Other men, of course. Then
these other men were born with something more
than “ the obligation to obey ? ” And White
is one of these other men, of course. This is
the arrogant domination which all Tories and
bigoted hierarchs assume to themselves. White
asserts that suffering humanity’s only chance is
to change its own attitude with respect to
existing laws. In other words, it is to accept
penury, exactions, extortions, persecutions, in-
justice, and even stripes, with humble thank-
fulness, with a meek confession that all these
tribulations are deserved, and with a glorifying
attestation that all existing evils are for the
best. More of White’s political ethics are :
Neither has popular government its foundation in what
might be called contributory rights. . . . The fact
that a man contributes towards the support of a State
gives him no right to a voice in the management of it's
affairs.
In other words, the fact that White contributes
towards the support of the State gives him no
right to take a hand in the management of the
State, still less does it give him a right to dic-
tate to others whether they shall interfere with
the State or not. Rut that isn’t what he means.
White’s idea is a theocracy, with W hite and
his co-thinkers to interpret the Theos. In
short, White goes in for a Despotism,
apparently on his own chosen argument
that the universe is a Despotism, and that God
is the governing despot, and therefore White,
as an assistant-GoD, should also be a despot.
Moreover, says this soul-slaughtering autocrat
of humanity :
Whatever may be the cause of pauperism, it is neces-
sary in a state of trial and preparation.
Mr. White repudiates the idea that Chris-
tians of “moral nerve” have any “sickly
sentimentality” about poor people or their
sufferings, “much less does he deplore or
lament their existence,” but rather rejoices
in it as a “field wherein to exercise and
develope and perfect his gifts and graces.”
The poor, argues this man of microscro-
pic “gifts and graces,” are born for the
very purpose of being “hewers of wood and
drawers of water,” in which fascinating occu-
pations they should “ find their chief satisfac-
tion and enjoyment.” If they don’t, they may
take it on the authority of Pastor White that
they are “ fighting against God.” Until
Moderator White spoke, last week, it would
generally have been considered impossible that
this enlightened time was graced by any one
man so hopelessly crazed with bigotry.

Amidst the shrieks of horror and indignation
excited by ’orrid details of the “Williams”
case, the still, small voice of common sense, re-
marking in a casual way that the principal was
a homicidal maniac, is seldom heard. The
world is already exulting over the certain
strangulation of Mr. Deeming (although its joy
is tempered by the sad reflection that he can't
be hanged several times over), and nobody
makes any allowance for ‘ ‘ the strange, fixed
stare,” the reckless braggadacio, and gratuitous
lying which, in the uncriminal human being,
are accepted as evidences of a mind diseased.
Deeming brings back to memory an old-time
comic song, entitled “ The Mad Butcher.” His
madness seems such a small matter as compared
with the enormity of his slaughterings that it is
not thought worth consideration, and the police
have strong personal objections to an enquiry
into the history of the Deeming family, which
might possibly lead to the discovery of several
uncles and aunts in lunatic asylums. Enough
for the purposes of justice that several of the
miserable wretch’s wives and offspring are being
discovered under hearthstones. Yet the very
motive of his crimes recommends Deeming to
the kind consideration of the medical faculty.
A man who slays a sequence of wives in order
that he can marry a fresh lot must be hopelessly
insane.
As an explanation of the absurdly light sentence
passed upon G. N. Taylor, it was, in the first
instance, alleged that Judge Hodges expected
other charges to be brought against the for-
tunate malefactor. Even if such were the case
the »“ emotion ” displayed by his Honor, and
the sympathetic manner in which he referred to
the prisoner’s painful position and bruised
“conscience,” have still to be accounted for.
Taylor’s career, from the time when he made
his first rise in life by failing for 4£d. in the £,
offers no evidence of the man having possessed a
conscience. Judge Hodges knew very little
about Taylor, apparently, was not even aware,
perhaps, that he had misappropriated £IOO,(KX>
in addition to the “valueless pieces of paper ”
that got him into trouble. But, by the same
token, the soft-hearted Justice couldn’t have
known that Taylor had a conscience, and why
he should take that article for granted for no
better reason than that the prisoner belonged
to the silk-hat brigade is a legal mystery.
Judge Hodges laid stress upon the fact that
he didn’t consider solitary confinement neces-
sary for a penitent gentleman like Taylor. A
list of the cases in which the same Judge has
ordered “ solitary ” would be interesting.
The democratic party, both in Victoria and
South Australia, is getting ready for the
approaching election in a headlong, bull-headed
manner which promises disaster. Every pro-
gressive league and democratic league and
labour league and socialist organisation is busy
nominating its own candidates with a hopeless
disregard of everybody save itself. Every
“ prominent man,” good, bad, and indifferent —
it doesn’t matter so long as he is “ prominent ”
—is apparently going to contest some electorate
or other, generally a city or suburban one.
Eur every constituency in the vicinity
of Melbourne there promises to be
half-a-dozen assorted democrats in the
field, while the remote country electorates
are being neglected. Not a single individual
of any note in connection with the progressive
party shows the slightest sign of standing out
and devoting himself to the work of organisa-
tion—which is far more important than the
mere business of getting into Parliament. The
old Conservative party, when it has a super-
fluity of candidates, and is likely to lose
through the splitting of the property-vote,
either persuades or pays the least promising
competitors to stand out—generally it has to
pay. The democratic party, being short of
money, can’t pay, and unless its super-
fluous candidates will retire from purely
disinterested motives they must all go to the
poll and the results will inevitably prove disas-
trous. And so far, unfortunately, none of the
objectionable candidates —the ones whose sole
recommendation is their powers of gas and voci-
feration—show any sign of retiring either from
disinterested or any other motives. The chief
hope of the party seems to be that its good men
will stand out and give the blatherskite section
and the persons who obtrusively spell God
with a small “ g” a fair chance —and even that
isn’t much of a hope when it is intelligently
considered.

Dipsomania is now recognised as a disease. It
is a misfortune that requires medical treatment
as assuredly as does epilepsy. A dipsomaniac
is intermittently as helpless as an epileptic.
Some savant has summarised his opinions on
alcoholic weaklings somewhat in this wise : —A
dipsomaniac is not a drunkard. A drunkard
will drink whenever, and as often as he can
get liquor ; a dipsomaniac will only drink when
his ailment is upon him. Dipsomania most
probably comes within the category of brain-
diseases. Like all other brain-diseases, its
origin, phases, and possibility of being com-
pletely eradicated, are yet matters of small
knowledge—and often of darkest ignorance—
with modern medical science. Hospitals for
inebriates have, however, been established in
most parts of the civilised world with notable
success. Such institutions combine humani-
tarianism with common - sense. There is
not living a dipsomaniac who would not
gladly surrender himself to their con-
trol during his dangerous periods. As for
the self-elected drunkard, gaol is the only
institution that can hold him back from
his beloved bestiality. A movement is- afoot
in Sydney —and not a day too soon—to estab-
lish at Gordon a “Cottage Home for Male
Inebriates.” The address of the honorary
director is 9 Princes-street, Dawes Point, Syd-
ney. The Bulletin recommends this move-
ment to public support, and will take charge of,
and duly forward to the promoters, any sub-
scriptions .which sympathisers may choose to
entrust to its temporary custody. A fund is,
of course, needed to initiate the scheme, but,
afterwards, the hospital should be to a large
extent self-supporting.

The Sydney Daily Telegraph welcomes with
both hands the issue of a cheap edition of “A Plea
for Liberty,” a compilation of arguments for
the perpetuation of wage-slavery—the writers
misname the thing, “Individualistic Liberty”
—and is so eager to floor the N.S.W. Labour-
party and Socialists generally with the volume,
that it doesn’t stop to read it. The reviewer
glared on the title-page, and, instantly infected
by his Single-taxer’s lust for the blood of the
Socialists, proceeded to stab ’em with quota-
tions from Herbert Spencer, who writes
the introductory chapter. Why didn’t the
D.T. quote the reference on page 138 to
Henry George (erstwhile its god) as an “ in-
coherent Freetrader ”? Why didn’t it chuckle
over the denunciations on another page of
George’s “ d priori vapourings ” ? Why didn’t
it quote the foot-note on page 29, which iden-
tifies no inconsiderable portion of George’s
writings as socialistic, and the allusion (also on
page 138) to “ the decisive rebuff” Australians
gave George? And why didn’t it—it never
faces a problem raised by democracy fairly and
squarely—inform its readers that ‘ ‘ A Plea for
Liberty ” is merely a plea for Anarchy ? Why ?
Because the D.T.’s logic is only that of the
capitalists’ ledger.

Thus Professor Jenks, ex-Law Lecturer to
Melbourne University on “ Federation,” in his
“ Government of Victoria ” :
Every community contains many persons whose import-
ance and possible emoluments may be lessened by any
surrender of its independence. Those who are responsible
for a particular policy fear for its continuance under the
new regime. Those who are now supreme may, after
union has come, be subordinate. The gain of the com-
munity may be their personal loss. So long as the com-
munity is apathetic, the influence of these men is
naturally great. And the community is busy about other
things, and prefers to shut its eyes to dangers ahead until
an appeal to its physical fears arouses its attention. Then
if the danger is vivid enough the community insists on
the obstacles being removed. If the French fleet were
once to strike a successful blow against the English on
the great ocean highways, the Federal Union of Aus-
tralia would be accomplished in three months. If no
such event happens, it may not be realised for 30 years.

The very latest —and truest—yarn about
Edward Blueskin Iloyce : “Toddy ” found a man
the other night lying in a Melbourne drain. A
crowd gathered, as Teddy proceeded to lift up the
unknown, and they said : “Oh, it’s only another
drunk.” “ Gentlemen,” protested Blueskin, “this
man is not drunk ; I’ve been a medical student,
and I know this is epilepsy. Now what man
present will put in a shilling with me for
a cab, and I’ll take him to the hospital?”
But as the Good Samaritan of burlesque beauti-
fully put it, “ they all went away at once.” So
Teddy paid the cab-fare, and lifted his charge
into the presence of one of the omniscient medicoes
at the hospital. “ H’m, he’s drunk,” said the
latter. “ Sir,” replied Teddy, “ he is not drunk ;
I’ve been a medical student myself, and I know
this is epilepsy.” “Ah, you’re Mr. Koyce, are
you not?” “The same; but don't you forget
that this is epilepsy.” And it was. Teddy sums
up, “There’s no country in the world so cruel to
its wayfarers as this wonderful Australia.”

“ Baron Swanston,” the man arrested in
West Australia on a charge of having, in Decem-
ber last, while masquerading under the name of
“Albert Williams” and several other aliases,
murdered his wife, Emily Lydia “Williams,”
whose mutilated body was found corded-up and
jammed in a bed of cement, and buried beneath a
hearthstone in an empty house in Windsor, Vic-
toria, on March 3, and who is also accused of
having, in August last, at Bainhill, Lancashire,
England, similarly butchered and buried a former
wife and-four children, is declared by the police
authorities to-be one Frederick Bailey Deeming, a
man of consistently evil reputation, who had pre-
yiously left behind him a trail sinister pretty well
all over, Australia (.with the exception, apparently,
of West Australia, “ Swauston’a ” refuge), Maori-
land, and South Africa.

The accompanying portraits will be seen
to differ strikingly from each other. The portrait
taken in gaol might, popularly be supposed to be
the more correct, inasmuch as. the gaol artist had
no incentive to flatter the, sitter ; nevertheless, ii),,
our, opinion, the official photographer has dealt
with him tire more tenderly. The gaol record
gives the height of Deeming at sft. 41,in., and
chronicles that he was born at Liverpool in 1854,
and arrived in Australia per Cotopaxi in 1881.
His eyes are blue and his hair liadit-brown.
The laboured efforts of the Press to invest the
accused man with certain attractive attributes, as
a means of intensifying public interest, evoke the
ridicule of Jhose who knew Deeming, and who re-
member him only as a commonplace, pilfering,
cantankerous, flash and vulgar person, inordinately
showy and desirous of notoriety. This trait was

remarked in “ Williams” on the Kaiser Wilhelm
11., and in “Swanston ” on the steamer to W.A.,
both voyages being marked by noisy fables of

solvency, a gentleman met Deeming in a train and
asked him whether he had seen an advertisement
enquiring after him in the “ Personal ” column of

On the Pam Plimsoli, in ’B2, Mrs. Deeming

was “sticking up to her.” On arrival in Sydney

children, murdered at Rainhill, were
born in Sydney, one at sea, and one in England.

The police theory is That Frederick
Bailey Deeming, Bailey, Mollett, Ward, Levey,
Lawson, Williams, Drown, Willows, Dobson,
Duncan, Dawson, and Swanston are one and the
same individual.
The whole case hinges on one question—ls
“Baron Swanston” the man who rented the
Windsor villa? Max Hirschfeldt, a fellow-
passenger by the ship, who has been sent to
Berth by the Victorian Government to identify
“Swanston” declares him to be the “Albert
Williams ” of the Kaiser Wilhelm XL, says
that he has lost three-stone in weight. “Swan-
ston” admits the recognition, but still declares his
innocence of the Windsor murder. His line of
defence is that he quarrelled with and left his
wife at the Federal Coffee Balace, on account of
her goings-on with another man. This is hardly
compatible with the fact that he still holds many
of her belongings. He defies the Melbourne
tradesmen to identify him, and asserts that “Mrs.
Williams ” was seen in Melbourne as late as 20th
December, the murder having been supposedly
committed on the 24th. The “ Williamses’ ’’ mar-
riage certificate has been found amongst his effects.
Accord ing to his own confession, he was an intending
bigamist, if not a murderer. He stood the unex-
pected appearance of Hirschfeldt with unmoved
equanimity. He “eats, smokes, and sleeps well,”
and “appears perfectly happy.”
A fellow-passenger says of “Mrs. Williams”
that she was “a, thoughtful, serious, solicitous,
ministering woman of 27,” and that “ a coarse
speech would have withered her like' am east
wind.”
What self-exculpating theory “ Williams ” will
put forward'for the Rainhill corpses that tell of the
murdfers of his-other wife and-children;. remains to
be seem.

heavily-manned and historically-costumed com-

enthusiastic gallery, and, the reasonable approba-
tion of a comfortably-filled- house. The personnel:
of the present, m.elo drama is more than satis-
factory from an, athletic point of view, and Mr,.
Glover, as Captain Vernon, R.N., of H.M.S..
Dauntless, looks as though he had stepped out of
some old-time canvas of the naval days of Colling-
wood and Jervis. Mr. Glenny’s Jack Westward
is, in the first,act more particularly, an ideal pre-
sentation , of the careless tar, whose exploits and
emotions were the theme of Dibdin’s songs ;.and
the Ben darker, “bos’un,” of Mr. Seagrave is a-
clever sketch of ante-steam navigation, reminiscent
of boyhood’s acquaintance with Captain Marryaf s
marine drolleries. Mrs. Holt, as Margery Briar-
wood, is an English , lass of traditional sweetness
and breezy delight; and that'clever little woman,
Miss Flora, Anstead, adds another British .dame,
to the “ Durdens ”of the past; The ever-Bland,
on the contrary, is too new for this production,
and, clever as he undoubtedly\ is, memories ofi
“New Babylon” will, force themselves, between .
the. moss-grown joints of his Joe Strawbones, the
jolly young waterman, whose merriment is of a,
Jacques-like brand i of, and whose,
pathos smacks of the Judaic satire of Seven Dials.,

The “Arabian Nights,’’ at
Sydney Criterion, gave place on Satur-
day last tq Tom Taylor and A.,W.
Dubourg’s three-act uompdy of “ New
Men arid Old Acres,” which afforded
an opportunity to Mr. Geo. W.
Anson to inaugurate a series of farewell
performances prior to his departure for another
and (alleged) better land. Mr. Anson’s
Bunter, “a self-made man,” is a richly
unctuoiis and financially ker-istian impersona-
tion, stuffed with misquotations of Holy Writ
and reeking with,the,monetary morality of the
>Stock Exchange; and. in make-up, dressing, and
oyery other conceivable detail, the ever-green
Ecdes sustained a reputation for artistic complete-
ness which has undergone the test of 200 nights of
banter fit the Court ,theatre, Ijondon. The
Samuel Brown of the present cast is Mr. E. Sass,
a frank, fearless and fatherly merchant from
Liverpool, whose well-tailored and athletic figure,
and carefully-trimmed beard and pointed mous-
tache, assist the belief that he is sustaining the
character of a French officer on a diplomatic
mission to, the Court of St. James, rather than
that of a mercantile millionaire of mortgaging
proclivities. Miss Annie Taylor upholds a con-
siderable section of popular delectation in the role
of Mrs. Bunter, with a versatile and altogether
wondrous wardrobe, and a unique vocabulary
inherited from her ancestress, Mrs. Malaprop.
Marmaduke Vavasour, Msq., of Cleve Abbey, in
white gaiters, is, in the person of Mr. Robert
Brough, duly and conjugally deferential to
the one brainy woman of the comedy
(Lady Matilda Vavasour), a character upheld bv
that princess of mothers-in-law, dowagers, and old
maids, Miss 10. R.omer. The acting of this lady
is artistic in the highest degree. Her conception
of the character is crisp and clean-cut to a nicety
and it is not too much to say that nothing better
in this line has ever been done on any Australian
stage. Mr. Chichester is a fairly adequate Bertie
Fitz-Urse, and Mr. Corlesse has surpassed his
average in the German mining - agent and
swindling financier, Berthold Blasenbalg ; or, as
ho is styled encouragingly by Bunter Anson
“ Blazey, old boy,” with much addition of mis-
applied Scriptural exhortation. The attorney
Seeker, is a dry brand of country lawyer, crisply
played by Mr. G. L. Gordon, and Mr. Findlay’s
Montmorency is an edition-de-luxe of plush-
covered servanthood. Miss Brenda Gibson plays
Fanny Bunter, and this part, with its gushing
raptures and fictitious sentimentalities, j g
altogether unsuited to a clever little actress whose
forte is rather in the quiet path of domestic
felicity and in the representation' of the “Sweet
Lavender ” type of heroine. In fact, Fanny
Bunter is about the one known part in modern
comedy that would have absolutely fulfilled all the
vocal and physical demands of Miss-Marie Fraser.
The present production.is noteworthy as affording
an opportunity to a London actress (Mrs.
Molyneux- —nee Harper) to make her Australian*
debut in the character of Lilian Vavasour, the
heiress of a vast deal of family pride
ancient traditions, and picturesque ruins of
the spotted Gothic variety. Mrs.. Molyneux
(a native of Maoriland, by the way) Jg.
tall, handsome, and graceful* with a sweet voice
strangely suggestive of Mrs. Brough’s, a fine stage
presence,, and a face capable of expressing a con-
siderable portion of the gamut of dramatic
emotion. From her entrance in the first act she-
was a favourite, and she gained hand over hand 1
upon the good graces of her audience, until* at* the-
fall of the drop,- everybody in. the house regarded*
her in. the light of an old stage acquaintance —and*
one can hardly say more for the success of a.
debut.

The Grand* Italian Opera Company at the
Sydney. Garrick, has contracted for another week's
supply of classic music, and fulfilled the conditions-
of illustrating a. line of Miltonls anent “ linked*
sweetness long drawn, out.” These Tuscan*,
artistes are. of a particularly active and energetic
variety, and pile vocal Pelion* upon instrumental.
Ossa with a persistency worthy the countrymen*,
of Julius Caesar* Last week no more than four
operas were presented, and 1 two were fresh pro-
ductions. The first of these was Donizettis grimt
and ghastly, albeit melodious, musical tragedy of.
“Luorezia Borgia,” with Signorina Guidotti ini
the title role, and Signor Vivalta* as Gennaro, her
tenor offspring. The character of Maffio Orsinij.
the contralto friend of the Venetian fisherman,,
was supported with a fair average of mezzo-
soprano success by Signorina Cavalleri, and*
the grandiose mightiness of Alfonso, the-
ducal potentate and velvet- clad poisoner of
Ferrara, was tremendously sung by. Signor Baldo
Travaglini, whose two magnificent* bass songs in*
the, second act were encored to the echo, as was-
also Signorina Cavalleri’s renderingof.“ll Segreto”'
in the final scene. The remainder of. the cast was
painfully English (or Australian), and the nasal*
and guttural Italian of these well-meaning voca-
lists sounded* for all the world like some variety
of a Woolloomooloo■ dialect, interspersed with*
fragments of* Chinese profanity. Mr. Hammill’s-
Gubetta was a peg above the average, but taking-
him all round the Australian is a worse Tuscan-
than he is an Imperial Britisher, anddiis voice is-
too often* excruciatingly suggestive of. the saw-
sharpening process. Signora Guidotti was not’-
exhilarating as the historical poisoner, and herr
singing of* Donizetti’s music was many degrees-
below par, while Signor Vilalta’s top-notes rasped**
their way through the auricular tissue with a**
brassy*energy, productive rather of headache than l *
{esthetic delight.. In fact, whatever of kudos
there was to be won in the recent production was 4
easily annexed by the ducal basso of Ferrara,
who, in.the person*of Signor Travaglini, looked-
every* inch a brigand land a gentleman.

On Friday night last, at the Y.M O.AV.
Hall, and again on the following Wednesday, Mr.
Alfred 1 ennyaon Dickens,, a well-dressed, well-
built, and admirably-upholstered son of his father,
delivered an alleged lecture, entitled “A Night *
with, Charles Dickens.” The audience was a poor
one on the first night, but it was probably as large* ■
as the lecture deserved, for Mr. A. T. Dickons has
nothing to tell that the world is not already
acquainted* with, neither is his method of telling
it sufficiently original to invite anything beyond a*,
passing spasm of very ordinary curiosity.

“As in a Looking Glass” was produced
B t Melbourne Opera House on Saturday before a
“ representative ” and thick-headed audience. The
performance was made to seem a genuine success
by the applause that followed each curtain-fall,
applause born of a dull determination to admire
Mrs. Bernard-Beere, but it was evident all the
time that sassiety didn’t appreciate the best
points of a play which seldom appealed to the
Kvuipathies of the proletariat. Adapted and
much altered from the novel of the same name,
“ As in a Looking Glass ” is a series of episodes in
the Career of a fragile enigma. The heroine is
definitely announced as a divorcee, subject to the
blackmailing of her co-respondent, Captain
Jack Fontinbras, but the exact measure of
Lena Despard’s impropriety subsequent to her
coing through the court, is not set forth
by the text. She may be either a fashion-
able courtesan, or a fairly respectable adven-
turess. Anyway, she keeps her eye always
fixed on the main chance, and when she gets hold
of any oof, the villain wants his whack. A goody-
goody young Scotchman, evidently intended for
the Church, is Lena’s particular “ mark ” through-
out the piece. As early as the first act she tries
to lure this verdant Caledonian into a loan, but
with characteristic prudence he declines to take a
hint on the subject of her financial complications,
whereupon the lady plots to break off his engage-
ment with a guileless girl and marry his vast
property herself. The aforesaid fiancee is easily
cleared out of the road, also the villain, who
obtrudes himself upon the situation in quest of
moneyas usual, is stalled off for a time by the
interposition of a certain chief of the Russian
secret police, an omnipotent individual, who
somehow happens to adore the heroine. The
young Scotchman, having meanwhile inspired a
holy passion in his battered Lena (probably on
account of his ingenuous snuffle and former stingi-
ness), falls an easy victim to the enchantress.
They are just married when the villain turns up
again, before the chief secret policeman can arrest
him, and makes a scene which ends in his lady’s
frank confession to her husband that she is not
quite unspotted before the world. The young
man turns from his love, w'ho retires to the library
to die. Then the potential “ peeler ” expostulates
with the shattered bridegroom, telling him that
he has really won a prize in the matrimonial
lottery, and all would end happily (the villain
having departed in a pair of handcuffs) but for the
rash behaviour of Lena, who has taken Rough-on-
rats and expired in great agony before forgiveness
could arrive.
# # *
Mrs. Bernard Beere’s short, but decisive,
death-scene was the one palpable hit of her inter-
esting performance, yet even in her supreme
effort she fell short of her high reputation. Less
testhetic and angular than of yore, she seems less
charged with electricity and nervous power. Mrs.
Beere, at the present moment, is a great actress
in a weak state of health, and will perhaps be
critically judged by people whose judgment didn’t
attempt to struggle against the fascination of Sara
Bernhardt’s more vigorous personality. Her
acting is delightfully natural, however, and will
grow upon the public taste as good work always
must. It is a pity that Mrs. Beere makes her
first appearance in a character which doesn’t
play itself in the smallest degree, but requires the
constant assistance of all her strength and skill to
enable it to hold the sympathy of the house.
Herbert Standing, as Captain Jack Fontinbras,
is an even more imposing person than he used to
be, and the part fits his hard man-of-the-worldly
style like a glove. The old familiar full flavoured
French accent of Marius will warm the hearts of
many who remember him only in comic opera,
and had no idea that the versatile Mons. could
play a rather melodramatic character, like that of
the Chief of Police, with such excellent effect.
Mrs. Beere’s two principal supports are very wel-
come strangers. Elsewhere the new company
shows no particular ability. Miss Henrietta
Watson is satisfactory as a very soft virgin, and
Otho Stuart, as the young Scotchman, is pain-
fully suggestive of Miss Achurch’s Charrington.
In the matter of personal appearance and clothes
Otho is about as nice as Percy Lyndal, but his
priggishness is distinctly of the Charrington
pattern and very trying. Harold Russell is hope-
lessly absurd in a stupid character —the rest get
no chance to make any difference one way or
t’other. The play is handsomely mounted, and
Mrs. Beere’s costumes are things to be seen.
** * *
“Wilful Murder” was an instantaneous
triumph at the Alexandra, inasmuch as the Crime
on the Hearthstone occurred in the first act, and
the innocent man was discovered in a very com-
promising position long before nine o’clock. There
he stood, by a new-made grave in front of the
grate—blood upon his hands, a corpse at his feet,
a bucket of cement in the middle distance, and
the fatal hammer in the background. It looked
real bad for the unjustly suspected one, but he
came out all right in the long run, and the
Deeming of the piece had to pay forfeit to the
law. Further details of this horrible tragedy can
be included in our next, because “ Wilful Murder ”
is a drama destined' to catch on and prosper,
Dampier has mounted it with that end in view,
and the savage interest of the story is enhanced
by a quantity of new and original scenery. The
cement used in the play is reported to have come
fmm the place where the Windsor hero procured
his mixture.
** * *
“Randolph the Reckless ” has been doing
moderately well at the Royal, notwithstanding
its inherent imbecility and specially weak spots.
On Saturday a few selections from the late panto-
mime were thrown in to give weight to the enter-
tainment, which may be expected to last out the
season. Next Saturday (26th) a mixed programme,
consisting of “ Good for Nothing ” and two acts
from “Jack Sheppard,” will be given for the
benefit of dear Billed Barlow, a damsel whose
early departure is looked forward to with red eyes
by hundreds of humble admirers.
** * *
The same night will bring a revival of
“Dorothy,” with Marie Halton in the name-part,
at the Princess’. There is no visible need for
withdrawing “ La Cigale,” but it goes out of the
bill in order that Elton may appear as Lurcher
once more before he passes hence.
** * *
“ Captain Swift,” at Melbourne Bijou, has
won the praises of the judicious, and promises to
be heard of again when times are more prosperous
and the B. & B. supporters are on their legs
again. It is a remarkable fact that the old
adherents of the Bijou are especially broke just at
present. The wave of depression caught them all
m a lump and swept them into the background,
where they are probably retrenching and dream-
uigof their faded glories. All the same, “ Captain
owift” is doing his second week to satisfactory
business. Mrs. Brough has aged her representa-
tion of the guilty mother since the opening night,
Whilst Titheradge and Lawrence have put a truly
brilliant polish upon their dramatic altercation in
the garden. “ Sophia ”on Saturday.
** * »
The halls are much as usual, except that
Pope and Sayles have taken over St. George’s
from Frank Clarke, and set up niggers in place of
silk stockings. Jeremiah Brown, the veteran who
threw a brick at Napoleon in the Battle of
Waterloo, has been receiving visitors at the
Cyclorama, and the Windsor Horror is a new and
fascinating item at the Waxworks.
** * *
The Melbourne Royal (otherwise “Aus-
tralian ”) Dramatic Company seems to have been
bounced into liquidation against the wishes of a
large number of shareholders, who are now alive
to the fact that other than press influences
were working for the annihilation of Coppin’s
enterprise. Had a poll been taken on the liquida-
tion question, it is tolerably certain that the
“ Noes ” would have silenced the malcontents by
a large majority. Instead of which, the manage-
ment remained passive whilst a handful of
possibly inspired growlers xnade arrangements to
carry the winding-up motion at their next little
meeting. And, when said motion was duly
rushed through, the directors threw up the sponge
forthwith, without making an effort on behalf of
the silent shareholders, who naturally expected
them to resist. Perhaps no other public company
with a fair prospect of success in the future was ever
thus easily snuffed out. The Royal management
had lost something under in six months,
two-thirds of the disaster being attributable to
their agent’s purchase of Laura Villiers and a
third-rate company. The importation of a fairly
capable crowd, in lieu of Miss Laura’s lot, would
have earned for the revived theatre a good name
and a decent profit on the half year. It is solely
on the strength, therefore, of a big initial blunder
(the result of blind trust in an agent whose
engagement has now expired) that this hasty
liquidation has been brought about. A huge
success for any new Melbourne theatrical venture
was impossible under present “depression” cir-
cumstances, but anyone can see that the case of
the Royal is far from hopeless. Apart from the
Villiers fiasco, the old theatre, which can cer-
tainty boast the best business “ stand ” in
the city, has been doing sufficiently well
to justify shareholders in holding on to their spec,
until times improve. With the retirement of
George Coppin the show is not necessarily bound
to collapse, in order that rival managers may
chuckle and the Age whoop for joy. Harwood is
just back again from Yurrup, loaded with the
latest dramatic lore, and if he were prepared to
wade in and take the management of affairs a
majority of shareholders in the A.D. Co. would
doubtless be glad to go for a recovery of their
money. If every Melbourne management had
decided to stand or fall by the past half-year’s
balance-sheet, which theatre wouldn’t be closing
its doors ?
At Poverty Point.
T!k drama and the actor were popular. He noticed
that the actor not only shouted very loud, but kept on
changing his key. Shortly afterwards he met one of the
supers., and asked why the actor shouted in such different
keys. “Oh! don’t you know the reason of that, sir?
That’s to keep the men to their work. When he changes
his key, it’s to show that the limelight isn’t on him.’
|> Mrs Bernard Beere has heard a lot about the behind
scenes luxury of Melbourne theatres. Unhappily she is
at the only barn, the Opera House, where desperate
attempts have been made to provide her with a lair by
Lohr. The disposition of the bunks is the greatest
mystery at that house, and Mrs. Beere has to pilch her
looking-glass over a saddlery shop.
Williamson writes to the Melbourne press complaining
of the unfavourable notices of Nellie Stewart being
quoted, and encloses a sheaf of others.
The Thespian has a vast experience of lodgings. In
England and America—only to a very small extent in
Australia—you find the boarding of theatricals made a
science by good dames devoted to it. The jocular trage-
dian and solemn low comedian are catered for, while the
prima donna’s whimsies are all remembered m the bill,
but no theatricals fancy playgoers for landladies.
“ As In a Looking Glass ” is one of those novels of the
miserable one-book author, as with “ Little Lord Fauntle-
roy ” “ Eoce Homo,” “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” “ Pilgrim s
Progress,” “ Tom Brown’s School Days,” and “ Robinson
Crusoe.”

viewer, that such a wicked play as “ The Dancing Girl
failed in Australia, but, as with “Captain Swift, the

Billee Barlow is a brunette, her almost black, silky hair

Cynics remark that they can get some of Carl Hertz’s
tricks, such as the vanishing bird-cage, “ out of a book
published by Routledge or somebody. Foolish dream.
You can get the book, and the bird-cage, and the canary,
and after twelve months of monotonous struggling with
the trick, you feel ashamed of yourself for having dragged
the black art so low.

J. C. Williamson doesn’t favour hotels in connection
with theatres. It is believed that had he his own way,
there wouldn’t be a hotel within a hundred yards of any
theatre he controlled. He is credited with the remark
that hotel-theatres are relics of the digging days-when
men wouldn’t visit theatres unless they were drunk.
W. H. Leake’s death is reported, from Adelaide. He
was a useful actor, but his ambition was restricted by his
possession of a physique not superior to Edmund Kean s.
Came from America as a kind of star, leading man, opened
at Melbourne Royal, in Bartley Campbell’s “ My Partner,
but gravitated to stock, though he had a big show as
Othello to Miln’s lago. Perhaps he was better as Mr.
Haviland in “Little Lord Fauntleroy," or Yank Thursby,
in “ Hans the Boatman.”

R, S. Smythe.

There was no romance and mystery about the dim past
of shaggy old Zerbini, it appears. London Figaro,
always well-informed concerning musical people and
events, refers to Zerb. as a once well-know viola, who
“ started in the Drury Lane band, afterwards working his
way up to the Monday Popular Concerts. On the death
of 'his wife his brain gave way, and eventually he emi-
grated to Australia.” There was a disposition amongst
Melbourne musical circlers to overrate Zerbini’s ability
because he didn’t wash himself very much, and to imagine
that he must have a strange, eventful history to account
for his disturbed brain.

Why should the first appearance of an English society
actress mean programmes smeared with a sickly scent?
Is there any connection between spicy tragedy and an
overpowering odour of cherry blossom? Mhy not “As
in a Smelling Bottle?”
The wise words of Florence, the American actor : “ My
dear One gallon of whisky costs about three
dollars, and contains nearly 65 fifteen-cent. drinks. Now,
if you must drink, buy a gallon and make your wife the
bar-keeper. When you are dry give her 15 cents, for a
drink, and when the whisky is gone she will have, after
paying fob it, $6.76 left, and every gallon thereafter will
yield the same profit. This money she should put away,
so that when you have become an inebriate unable to
support yourself and shunned by every respectable man,
your wife may have money enough to keep you until your
time comes to fill a drunkard’s grave. Yours truly, W. J.
Florence.”
A member of the company which David Christie Murray
took with him to England writes to contradict pretty
nearly everything that The Bulletin has said about that
person. According to this letter, D.C.M. isn’t doing a
successful tour with his new play, “Ned’s Chum;” he
grew tired of the stage almost as soon as he reached
England, and peacefully abandoned a crowd of enraged
Thespians to fight for themselves—in fact, judging from
the forcible language which our correspondent applies to
the “Joseph’s Coat” man, it would seem that he had
simply thrown them out and locked the door on them.
Fred. Stanmaur is now taking the company round under
difficulties, and trying to save them from utter wreckage,
and they fill in their spare time praying that Providence
may drop a cathedral on Murray and wipe him out.
Lily Dampier has had a Queensland gold mine named
after her. The name, has brought good luck, too. It is
almost the only promising mine in the Cook district.
A theatrical manager is defined as “a person with
money enough to pay salaries to a company, or nerve
enough to owe them.”
Premier Shiels’ Casterton speech last week
was an eloquent oration, lasting from a few
minutes after 8 p.m. until nearly midnight, and
was listened to without one adverse interruption.
The Melbourne Agk report of it occupied 10£
columns.
Premier Shiels, of Victoria, has utterly
“ brushed away the cobwebs of fiction which
crafty and unscrupulous political spiders have
been spinning round ” the question of One-man-
one-vote, by representing it as the outcome of
dictation on the part of the Trades Hall. Shiels
proves historically that the single-vote question
“came to the Munro Government as it did to the
Shiels Government as a legacy from past Govern-
ments ” :
It was forced on to the Government by the question of
Federation. Plural voting was always considered an
excrescence upon the Constitution. The law of our Con-
stitution is that man, and not property, shall be repre-
sented.
Then Mr. Shiels went on to show that the Conser-
vative Government of 1873 —embracing such Tories
as J. G. Francis, Edward Langton (noV a dual-
votist!), Justices Stephen and Kerferd, Edward
Cohen, Alex. Fraser, and Robert Ramsay—pro-
posed a Single-vote Bill, which the Legislative
Assembly passed “without a single voice being
raised against it.” The Berry Government of 81
proposed two Single-vote Bills, one for the
Assembly and one for the Council. The Gillies-
Deakin Ministry included in its Electoral Reform
Bill clauses abolishing the plural-vote and insti-
tuting the single-vote. An amendment, limiting
the single-vote to one constituency, but permitting
property-owners to record a second vote in a fresh
* constituency, “ was defeated by an overwhelming
majority, and the single-vote was then passed
without a division.”

runs in another direction. He has dainty little feet and
