Toole is getting better. At latest advices the half-dead
Old humourist had tied, with infinite labour, a string to
his nurse’s chair, so that he can jerk it away when she is
going to sit down, and leave her on the floor. It is so like
the mirthful Toole—all his jokes are built very much that
way.
It’s “ Mr. Spong ” on the Melbourne Opera House pro-
grammes, but plain “ John Hennings.”
How supremely fortunate for Australia that Deeming,
alias Williams, never met Marie—but we stop here.
Howard Vernon is the modest violet of our stage. No
parts are more carefully studied or mere artistically
played than his, and yet his “jam ” is doled out in infre-
quent dribbles.
The Wild-Cat Column.
The thanks of the world are due to the N.S. W. managers
who write the mining reports, and to the papers that
print them. Of one mine it was said the other day that
“ the whip-horse having become lame a new one has been
obtained/' A mine with two whip-horses must be a big
thing. A horse-whip for the manager might also be
obtained. Another manager conceals nothing from the
public, as is shown by his report: “Am using cowhide
buckets in the winze and they are working well.” There
are some managers who would basely keep the cowbide
buckets dark and so keep down the price of scrip. There
are several mines passing through what the bosses
uniformly describe as “kindly rook,” which might do a
service some day by falling in. Dividends may be looked
for in a certain mine, for the manager has put his name
to a report stating that “the truck road has been laid
and is working smoothly.” It is all right when you have a
smooth truck road. “ Keeping the water down by one
man at the windlass ” may be rend as a neat compliment
to the windlassman or that the mine is not a deluge. A
certain manager having once reported that he had
“driven eleven feet towards the Broken Hill lode,” the
directors requested him to state how far the mine was
away from that great property whose lode they w'ere all
so anxious to strike. He innocently replied less than five
miles. This was too much. They removed the manager
instead of shifting the mine.
• * •
Hillgrove has a black-list of mines. The remark
made by a mining man, three years ago, that the payable
mines of the field could always be counted on the" fingers
of one hand, was prophetic. Baker’s Creek is a good
mine, spasmodic in dividends because of the difficulty of
keeping on the shoot of gold with so small a lode. Sun-
light is another good property with a big lode —and a
still bigger handicap, in the W’ay of a 10 per cent, grass
royalty to the owners. The Hopetoun is a payable mine,
of which the former price of the shares indicated great
expectations. Then come the mines which have passed
through great tribulation—Baker’s Creek, North and
South are dead, the former resurrected and again dying.
The Cosmopolitan died, was quickened into life and again
died. Some mines have many lives. Golden Gate,
Baker’s Creek Extended, North Eleanora, and others
whose very names have faded away, are all dead. Lady
Carrington and Garibaldi seem both approaching second
death. Hopetoun, South and North, Lady Hopetoun,
and other Hopetoun pups are sickening, and on the verge
of the grave. The Baker’s Creek Consolidated, seemingly
trying to live on reports. Why don’t they crush ?
* # #
A shareholder wants to know what has become
of the Ennybre Consols mine, New England? It has
fizzled out. Its history is brief. A mysteriously rich
trial-crushing in Sydney, consequent flotation (capital
£200,000), shares boomed, particularly in Adelaide, up to
12s. Thousands spent in plant, continuous newspaper-
puffing concerning rich developments during erection.
Plant completed, crushing commenced, big yield antici-
pated, Armidale shareholders delighted, plates “looking
well”—carrier-pigeons engaged to bring results. All
Armidale looking up skywards for pigeons. Crushing
“postponed.” Knowing ones get out. Finally, result
about an ounce ; manager blamed ; next crushing under
an ounce ; liquidation, plant sacrificed, new company;
short-lived. Mine abandoned, shares worthless.
# * *
Butchers’ reef has much the same history,
except there were no pigeons other than shareholders.
The Armidale, Uralla, Walcha, Glen Innes, and Tamworth
people have been badly plucked.
» * •
The auctioneer who submits forfeited shares on
Fridays at Richardson and Wrench’s, Sydney, is, we are
convinced, not a lover of that commodity. The cold,
callous way in which he offers thousands of shares which
not long ago represented as many pounds, is enough to
draw tears from shareholders. “Is there any offer?” is
repeated until the secretary meekly offers a penny.
Nobody follows, and the reserve being twopence, they are
passed in onlv to re-appear at the next sale.
# * #
What has become of the bulk of the Australian,
and particularly of the N.S.W. “ mining experts?” Have
they returned to the avocations from which in the boom
they emerged ? They are not in gaol—although they have
ruined ten times more people, by means of misrepresenta-
tion, than all the building society directors already there.
Results demonstrate that the mining expert reported him-
self either a rogue or a fool. To illustrate : it was at all
times useless to issue a prospectus, unless the accompany-
ing report from the mining report indicated dividends.
Hundreds of prospectuses were so issued, hundreds of
properties were floated, and the expert’s recommenda-
tions carried out to the letter. Now, there are very few
dividend-paying mines in N.S.W., and no N.S.W. expert
ever reported on any of them ; therefore, we say the
experts have retired on a shockingly bad average. Had
they invested money on their own recommendations, they
would be all ruined men. That would be consoling. In
this they alone revealed expertness. They often escaped
liability by the honorarium of a paid-up interest, and
declined participation in contributing shares on principle.
« * «
The Bulletin foreshadows that the next mining
movement will be in the direction of hydraulic sluicing,
the real potentialities of which few people appear to
understand. True, tens of thousands have been wasted
in this direction. The three great requirements for this
system of mining are an everlasting abundance of water,
a certain get-away or tail race, and a little gold. Un-
doubtedly more money has been thrown away on account
of the first two, than because of the absence of gold.
You cannot shift with water hills of alluvial unless the
water can carry them elsewhere. Hydraulic sluicing
means the doing of everything by water and nothing by
hand.
# * #
In America to-day there are dividend-paying
sluicing companies whose wash does not return two grains
to the load, and in many cases tens of thousands have
been expended in bringing in the water. The banks of
the Shoalhaven River are supposed to be by far the
greatest alluvial deposit in Australia. Here men have
been ruined generally in attempting to bring water where
it would not run on-to the ground for which there was
insufficient get-away. By the light of their disastrous
results it is unfortunately judged. Some day it will be
worked on a system commensurate with its value,
# # *
Liars may be divided into three classes : liars,
adjective liars and mining-experts.
if * #
After a couple of years’work, the Victory Co.,
of Charters Towers, have come across the celebrated
Brilliant reef. The Co. while proceeding to this con-
summation paid all expenses and also good dividends out
of the old Victory reef, to work which the company was
originally formed. In sinking on the underlie to con-
nect with the Brilliant Co.’s workings adjacent, 214 J tons
of stone were raised out of the shaft which has yielded
14920z5, melted gold, or about 7ozs, to the ton. This is
one of the biggest things in the mining history of
Queensland. It is recorded that the Victory Co. (50,000
shares at £1) was floated by Mr. C. J. Buckland, F.G.S.,
who lately came out from England chiefly for
the purpose of being present at the striking
of the Brilliant reef in Victory ground. The
Brilliant has been steadily paying 30s. per annum pet-
share on 50,000 (or £75,000 per annum) for the last couple
of years. The Victory, also, has now- a splendid prospect
ahead, and its shares have taken a big jump, both here
and in London, though they had long been comparatively
high in price, owing to the nearing prospect of getting
the Brilliant reef.
• # #
By the way, it was through Mr. Buckland’s
persistence and enterprise that the once-celebrated Sunny
Corner was developed as a silver-mine, in the face of great
opposition, when it had failed as a gold-mine. Mr.
Bnckland’s success in this direction (before the Barrier
had been heard of) was the first really big step in
Australian silver-mining.

POLITICAL POINTS
The N.S.W, railway service employs 12,500
persons.
Or. H. Reid is politically dead. Don’t you
see that he has one eye already.glazed ?
The Victorian Young Gentlemen’s Patriotic
League continues to make itself ridiculous.
Syme’s weekly, The Leader, says that the
Argus, in its defence of Speight, “excels itself in
Unscrupulousnoss and mendacity.”
Premier Playford (S.A.) writes;—“A
visit to India is an education in itself.” Then a
visit to India is just what Playford needed.
Every constituency in the Melbourne
metropolitan area is to he contested by a Labour
candidate at the coming Victorian elections.
Some of the country papers are getting
close to it. A Moruya (N.S.W.) journal speaks
of a vote for manhood and a vote for “ thirft.”
Adelaide politicians rumour that next
Session Chief Secretary Kingston, the one really
able man of the Ministry, will become Premier.
Bananaland is becoming convinced of the
necessity of establishing a Leper Hospital of its
Very own. The trail of the Pigtail is over us all.
Dan. O’Connor, M.L.C., seldom speaks in
the N.S.W. Council, except to prepare the way
for his resurrection in West Sydney at next
general election.
Hoolan, M.L.A., (Q.), declai ■es that the
renewal of the Black Labour question will lead to
bloodshed. The Bulletin would sooner see civil
War than slavery any day.
It is expected that 50 out of the 72
members of Queensland Assembly will suppoit
Griffith’s black slavery scheme, but that the
minority will stonewall for a dissolution.
According to Premier Shiels, the Victorian
Government expenditure has been increased by
*£3,137,000 within four years. “ Every 8J persons”
[i.c., adults) carry a Civil Servant on their backs.
The “ Y'oung Victorian Patriotic League,”
Up to date, has only held meetings in the strong-
holds of Capital, around Toorak. Wait till they
try Collingwood, Fitzroy, Richmond, ,or North
Melbourne !
A healthy sign is that at the Victorian
A.N.A. annual conference, at Sale, on 11th inst.,
almost all the speakers went straight in favour of
one man*one-vote and scoffed derisively at the
idea of “ an extra vote for Thrift.”
Melbourne Age now advocates the Swiss
system of electing Ministers from all parties as
against the present vogue of “ Government by
contradiction.” The Bulletin appreciates imita-
tion as the sincerest form of flattery.
G. H. Reid, leader of the N.S.W. Opposi-
tion, is addicted to the habit of addressing a
public meeting consisting of one Sydney Daily
Telegraph reporter. Wonder whether the “ rep.”
11 takes it as read,” and “collars the manuscript?”
Premier Shiels’s programme (Vic.) ; This
year—economy and taxes to make both ends meet;
next year—tax on unimproved values of lands;
third year—One-man-one-vote reform. It sounds
like a parlour game at “This year, next year,
now’, never.”
Berry is keeping pitch-dark in Melbourne.
At time of writing he had not been heard from
even through the reporters for two weeks. The
one effort since his return was a singularly foolish
endeavour to boom the almost paralysed Mer-
cantile Bank.
In the bye-election for Nowlunga (S.A.), a
farming district, the electors refused to let the
labour candidate say a word. This is a reaction
caused by the excited utterances of some of the
extremists of the Labour-party being taken too
seriously by the bucolic intellect.
Under Griffilwraith’s Kanaka scheme, how
much is the Polynesian Immigration Department
going to cost the Bananaland Government, seeing
that that Government proposes to control the
trade ? More bleeding of the white workers for
the purpose of importing labour to compete
against them.
The S.A, Local Optionists, flushed with
recent successes at the Licensing Benches, openly
announce that they are going straight for the
total prohibition of beer, wines and spirits. They
are going to root up the splendid vineyards !
The Bulletin will die, battle-axe in hand, in the
breach at Hardy’s claret-patch.

Queensland black-labour advocates assort
that the sugar industry has “cost the Government
of that province nothing to initiate or support it.”
Gehazi ! It has cost the province hundreds of
thousands sterling in inflated boom prices ; it lias
cost the white workers as much more in reduced
wages ; it has cost Bananaland its white reputa-
tion and branded it as a slave-state—and anything
that has cost all these losses and wrongs has cost
the Government irreparable damage and irrecover-
able penalties.

Dear Bulletin,—W. O. Hodgkinson,
senior member for Bourke, N.Q., says that
“minerals are like women’s eyes—they sparkle,
and are powerful to attract, but very often de-
ceive.” He might have extended the parallel to

man, One-man-one-vote, and a Great Republic
by-and-bye,” used to be W. O. Hodgkinson’s cry.
But look at him now ! an advocate of nigger-
labour ! Will he dare at next election to face the
Gulf music in store for him !

John Davies, M.L.C., has compelled the
derelicts of humanity and (N.S.W.) politics to
bend the knee and hail him as “a useful man.”
He is far and away the best underground engineer
in the nominee Council. Two or three years ago,
when he sneaked back into that congregation of
capitalists “ without a stain on his character” he
found himself an Ishmad. He lived down the
displeasure of the wealthy mutes, and to-day,
politically speaking, his support in the Council is
worth the votes of twenty men in the Assembly to
Parkes.

W. B. Perceval, the excited youth who
has just been appointed Agent-General for Maori-
land, is suffering from swelled head, partly owing
to his new dignity and partly because he had an
ancestor who was a British Prime Minister and
was shot in the House of Commons. At latest -
advices he had applied to the Imperial Govern-
rnent “to furnish a man-of-war at Sydney to
convey the Earl of Glasgow to Wellington.” if
the U.S.S. boats are not good enough for Glasgow
then that long, sad, murky Presbyterian had
better go across by raft. Perceval is already
going the way of other Agents-General; he was ‘a
democrat a month ago; he is a moderate Liberal
now ; he will be a solemn Tory in a year’s time;
and in three years he will be a howling reactionary,
a dude, a groveller, a K.C.M.G., an Imperial
Federationist, and 24 different kinds of a flunkey,

Ihe nobility and gentry of Toorak (Melb )
arc troubled in their minds-about the coming
election. Matthew Davies, knight, their old
member, is about to hide his diminished head in
private life, and they want to make sure of a
‘‘ worthy successor ”to that extinct volcano. At
a recent council of war the opulent Wolfe Fink
pointed out the dangers of too much competition
at the poll. What they had to fear, observed
the Fink, was that with two or more .jcntlc-
men contesting the election they might find
at the last moment that a Trades Hall candidate
would come forward. Even Toorak isn’t safe
these democratic times. As the district becomes
more thickly peopled it is a melancholy fact that
“ the electors are, many of them, owners of small
properties,” and people of that sort are not to be
relied upon. They may run in a real live radical,
on the revolting assumption that lie is a worthy
successor to a titled person like Davies.
G. IT. Wise, Directorial President of the
A.N. A., Vic., admits the future possibility of
“Australia one day becoming an independent
nation,”and thus expresses his feelings thereon:
If that day should ever come, and Oud (/rant that it be
a Ion;/ way off (I), we may still be able to say, “ God bless-
Great Britain and long lh*e the Queen.”
Certainly, independent Australia will be a free*
country, and anyone with a taste that way may
yell, “God bless Siberia, and long live the Czar
or “Cod save Satan, and may Gehenna endure-
for ever.” But the man who stands on a mast-
head as a patent Australian patriot, and High.
Cockalorum of an alleged National movement,,
and then hopes that National Independence may-
be “a lemg way off,” is a eunuch amongst his
kind, ana is no more an Australian at heart than
is any colonial Governor who comes out to
Australia as a bastard reflection of a Royalty
whose glimmer is obscured by the curvature of a
hemisphere.
The Melbourne Evening Standard cheer-
fully remarks that “ the industrious Japanese will
be a necessity in Australia if there is to be any
large development of our viticultural industry.”
The average daily paper, nowadays, when it
wants to boom a new industry, always starts by
remarking that it will be necessary to import
some foreign heathen or other to work it. If the
mining business is duM, tflere is a demand for
cheap Chinamen; when sugar gets low, the
Kanaka is the only person who can give it a fresh
start; if there is a weak, forlorn, broken-backed
feeling about the coal-trade, or the wine-trade, or
the silkworm industry, the Japanese, or the
Italian, or the Coolie is the only remedy. And
just here The Bulletin has a suggestion to make.
The evening newspaper industry is pretty weak in
Melbourne just now, and perhaps a large importa-
tion of Japanese editors and business managers
and cheap Chinese reporters would give it a fresh
start. Anyhow, if the only remedy for all other
branches of business depression is to sack the
white man and put Kanakas in his place, we don’t
see why the white pressman who recommends the
remedy shouldn’t have his own nostrum applied
to himself.

Miss Edwards tells, in February Fortnightly,
that Madame B. (then Miss Barbara Smith) spent
a day with George Eliot and George H. Lewes
shortly before they entered upon their unconven-
tional union. The great novelist that was-to-be
asked o nmsel of her friend. “'What right had I
to a. vise?” said Madame Bodiehon to Miss
Ed w< rd l “ I told her her own heart alone must
decide, and that, no matter what happened, I
would stand by her while I lived !” Magnificently
did she redeem her promise.
Dear Bulletin,— Reading your grim and
ghastly story of 12th inst., about the idiot girl
whom her rescuers found lying iu the. bush, in.
course of being eaten alive by ants, recalls to mind
another gruesome story (for which the Palmer
Chronicle was responsible, some years ago) :
“A. Nicholson informs us he came across a bull,
15 miles from the homestead, almost mad, as well
he might be, with the awful burden he had to
bear. It was the dead and putrid body of a baby
black, impaled on the horns of the beast. How
long it had been there is a,mystery, but it
ultimately dropped off. It is supposed that the
blacks surrounded the cattle, when the bull
charged, and in breaking away dashed through
the threatening circle, goring the child, and
bearing it away to a terrible death.” —F.
What People Say.
W. SIMMONS, ESQ., BENDIGO, (VIC.)
Writes, under date September 19, 1891 :
“ Dear Sir,- For the last four years 1 have
suffered great misery from nervous debility, com-
plicated with liver and kidney disease. My water
was scalding hot, and very scant and muddy. I
had sharp, shooting pains all over my body, was
very \yeary, always tired and languid, with no
appetite, and frequent attacks of vomiting and
headaches. I arose in a morning more tired than
when I went to bed, and was so constipated that
1 Lad to take the most violent cathartics. I cou-
su,ted doctors without number, and all without
benefit, until my wife persuaded me to use
Clements Tonic. Ihe first three bottles did not
seem to llave nil,cl ‘ effect . but after that I seemed
to get relief daily, and a three months’ course so
completely restored my health, that now I feel as
lf 1 had been born again or transplanted to an
entirely new sphere of existence. I was never so
well before, and feel as strong as a lion, and can
perform any reasonable amount of exertion with-
oufc batigue. You can easily understand how
grateful 1 am to your wonderlul remedy, for it is
wonderful that it should have cured my case,
which had baffleii the best medical skill these
colonies afford. Long live Clements’Tonic ! say
I; others »»«y doubt its value if they like. I
have proved it genuine.
• his case is similar to hundreds of others. The
patient consulted the doctors, who tried the usual
experiments, and each one adopted a different
treatment, not one being used long enough to
have any effect; finally, on a proper course of
Clements’ Tonic the patient recovered, as do all
others.

The late J. H. Carden, N.S.W. journalist,
although an Anglican churchman, was an enthu-
siastic Home Ruler. He once got a press invitation
to a Broken Hill Orange social gathering. Having
a good voice he was called on for a song, and pro-
ceeded to give “ The Wearing of the Green. In
the silence that followed you could have heard an
elephant drop.
Dr. George Kingsley, who died suddenly in
London last month, was the younger brother of
Charles Kingsley and was joint author with the
Earl of Pembroke of that most delightful book
“ South Sea Bubbles,” one of the three best books
yet published south of the line.

The death of Mrs. Ann Petrie, daughter
of Flinders, just cabled, removes the direct ink
between the present generation and that illus-
trious band of navigators who revealed to the
world Australia as we know it to-day. Her
decease relieves both the Victorian and New
South We’sh pension-lists of trifling annuities. As
an illustration of how even persons in the happiest
position for learning the details of historical
events may err, it may be mentioned that Mrs.
Petrie was a firm believer in the truth of the
pathetic story that Flinders’ book, containing the
record of his voyages and discoveries was issued
from the press the very day he died. Asa matter
of simple fact, the volume was issued fifteen days
before his death, and Mrs. Petrie herself uncon-
sciously supplied to the present writer the
evidence which enables him to make tins asser-
tion.

Another is added to the long list of jealous
murderers. William Stone, of Condobolm,
NS W loved Alice Anderson. So did Deor 0 c
Ryan. Miss Anderson favored Ryan, and went
out riding with him on St. Patricks Day. Sterne
ambushed for the pair, and, suddenly reveahn
himself, pointed a gun at the girl, whose horse
bolted and saved her life. Ryan dismounted and
laid hold of the gun, but, in an ensuing struggle,
was riddled with shot and died within two hours.
Stone, if caught, will be hanged, probably. But
hanging Stone, so long as the primal passion of
the human race remains the same, will deter no
wretch whose love goads him to frenzy.
Dear Bulletin, —If the Windsor murder
proves one thing more than another, it is that
“Conscience” is a moral disease, a mental
marasmus, an artificial psychological debility,
traceable to early training, and perhaps slightly
hereditary. It may be laid down as a broad rule
that a strong-fibred mind never owns a Conscience
until its owner is found out in some crime that
entails a severe punishment. there are some
minds so cheerfully balanced that they actually
forget altogether the crimes that they have com-
mitted, if those offences remain undetected, it
requires quite a forced effort of memory to recall
criminal commissions without some outside am.
Who has not felt this in a more or less mild way
at some time or other of his life? Time, undis-
turbed, is Lethe. Let memory be made to search
the unsavoury secrets of past personal history,
and every honest self-examiner will admit this
to his own mental tribunal, though no power
on earth may be able to make him con-
fess it publicly. This, of course, generally
for lesser offences than murder, but the
argument is effective for murders also; its com-
plementbeing only a question of degree. ihe
appalling nature of any crime is only haunting and
terrifying to the extent that the moral disease of
Conscience is present in the offender s mental
nature. Disguise the unpleasant fact how we
may, a murderer often eats as well, enjoys himself
as thoroughly, and sleeps as calmly, as the man
who “wouldn’t hurt a tty.” That is, so long as
he believes he is safe from public execration and a
penal doom. When he is caught, and when his
physical senses are confronted with a visible terror,
his physical nature betrays distraught symptoms.
Also when he is not quite secure from detection,
though he has not yet been caught; given absolute
security, and many men arc untrustworthy
monsters. Back-blocks life reveals this (especially
with niggers) every day, in the cases of men who
under civilised rule, are gentle, peaceable and
law-abiding. It is this common mental idiosyn-
crasy which makes the lash and capital punish-
ment both barbarous and inefficient. Until
detection becomes visibly probable, or actually
consummated, criminals are easy-minded and even
jaunty,—Yours, The D.

The joys of the domestic hearth have been
snug by many a poet. But, since the advent of
Mr. “Williams,” how about the joys of the
domestic hearthstone ?

Extract from a last week’s alleged letter
from Miss Rounsviile, the Windsor heroine, to her
sister
We intend going to see the detectives off and after-
wards to the waxworks, and I suppose we will be well
supplied with opera tickets. The Government is paying
all expenses. What a mockery, my wedding clothes ! I
was looking at them last night.
Same old sex! Weddings—opera—“free seats”
—gore !
* * *
“ Come Where Your Love Lies, Deem-
ing.”—Part song, revised.
* # *
The Royal Hotel, Bathurst, N.S. W., where
Swanston, the alleged murderer, put up, is the
noted old-time hostelry which, in Mrs. Black's
time, appeared at least in two novels and a hun-
dred police cases. The previous tenant before
present host Harley, was “Charley” Turner,
father of bowler Turner. A hundred years hence,
a cicerone will say to guests “doing’’the show-
places of Bathurst. “ ’Ere yer are, gents. ’Ere’s
where the Industrious Turner, ’im as wot murdered
seventeen women two hundred year ago, slept;
an’ ’ere’s where the notorious Swanston, the
cricketer, who bowled Grace, took ’is whisky !”
* # *
An exchange says that the Victorian
Government has rented 57 Andrew-street,
Windsor, the house in which “ Mrs. Williams ”
was murdered and bricked in. By the way, why
should the Victorian Government kill Deeming
in the event of his conviction ? Why shouldn’t
it, in the present depressed state of the revenue,
exhibit him, at half-a-crown a time, all over
Australasia and England? This would be far
more popular than cutting down the wages of the
Civil Service.
# * *
The Melbourne pressmen have made the local
Cathedral Hotel their headquarters : “ The nearer
the church the further from God.” Alleged mur-
derer Williams stayed there : the nearer the press
the further from publicity.
• * *
The Williams-Rounsefell affair demon-
strates for the millioneth time the absurd fashion
in which women run after any man supposed to
have money. It shows the defect in woman’s
education and opportunities—she is at present
totally dependent and has no chance except to
“ freeze on ” to a man.
# # *
At Yass, a man named Pickles was
arrested on suspicion of being the Windsor mur-
derer.—
What may have inspired a demon with the
idea of the Windsor fireplace murder very pro-
bably was the hazed recollection of that grisly
story by Wilkie Collins, entitled “ The Haunted
Hotel: a Tale of Modern Venice.” Therein a
woman —Wilkie was strong in producing female
monstrosities —poisoned her hubby (or paramour),
dismembered him, and buried the remains beneath
the hearthstone of an ancient palace on the
Adriatic, used at the time of the story as a modern
hotel. The mantelpiece was a marvel of horrible
ingenuity connected with the hearthstone. A
slight pressure on the occiput of one of the carya-
tides that supported it caused the stone to swing
aside and disclose the secret which a phantom
skull and bony fingers had vainly sought to tell
before to each guest of that queer bedchamber,
and the identification by a London dentist of the
artificial teeth in the victim’s skull “ gave away ”
the rest.
m * *
All the lynx-eyed official vigilance of
Darlinghurst prison is unable to keep out sen-
sational stories of successful villainy. Novels, in
the strict meaning of the term, are excluded, but
in serial form, as well as in short and pithy narra-
tive, they are accessible in such papers as
Cassell’s Saturday Journal and the Boys’ Own
Book, and many a crafty scoundrel has his educa-
tion improved by the perusal of “ How I Started
a Bank,” “The Glenmutehkin Railway: How I
got into It and how I got out of It,” and the
biographies of observant and philosophic thieves.
• • *
The Argus (whose cable-service is shared
by the S.M. Herald) “ did ” the Age badly over
the Windsor sensation It was smart of tho
ancient one to cable “Home” to its particular
prevaricator to hunt up the mother of Mrs.
Williams and get details. The Argus was a day
ahead again in supplying news of the discovery
of the bodies of four children and a woman, under
the kitchen floor of Williams’ house at Rainhill,
“in one red burial blent”— as the STANDARD
poetically puts it. But then, of course, the Age
had to keep both eyes on Speight.

and wished the arrest to be put off till next day !

What was the use of Mr. Woore, Coroner
at Sydney, dragging out that cancer-case so?
When the case comes for trial, the counsel who
defends Hood will simply ask the practised sur-
geons who give evidence, “ Have you ever
operated for epithelioma of the lip?’ “Yes.”
“You have been successful in many cases?”
“Yes.” “And you have also been unsuccessful
in very manv cases?” “\es.” And then the
band will begin to play and the case will practi-
cally be at an end. For no man can positively
swear the woman could have been cured.
# # #
The Bulletin lately remarked on the fact
that on a certain occasion during the tour of
Sheffield’s team the English professionals did not
come out of the same gate on the cricket-ground
as the amateurs. This,unspeakably odious, not to
say contemptible, .custom is maintained down
South, where a well-known local professional (Jim
Phillips, of the M.C.C.) comes out of the stand-
gate, while the—haw—gentlemen come out by the
members’ pavilion. “I hate fashionable Vic-
torians,” wrote Francis Adams lately—“ they are
such snobs. They are very little better than
English people,”
# * *
There is an unconscious touch of humour
in an “In Mernoriam ” advertisement about a
departed pugilist in a Melbourne paper last
week : —“ Oh, for a touch of that vanished hand!”
It recalls Frosty Face Fogo’s account of a
brotherly meeting between the Tipton Slasher
and Balloon, in a back bar. “ All of a suddent,
when they wuz by theirselyes, talking Quite
affectionate, we heerd a smashing of heverythink
like the Day of Judgment, and in five minutes
they wuz hamicably smoking their churchwarden
pipes again.”
* * *
And here, by the way, is a queer aspect of
the Australian “ In Memoriam” custom. Afashion-
able bachelor official is privately on terms with a
lady whose residential address —like her yellow
hair —speaks for itself. He dies, and every year
on the anniversary of his departure she spends
five shillings in letting the world, per medium of
the “leading ” daily, into the dead mans little
domestic secret and telling people how much she
misses her dear friend Reginald.
# # *
A certain widely-known Maoriland cele-
brity has been patiently waiting for 16 years for
admission to the London Carlton Club. He was
to have come up for election just as the mail left,
but unfortunately certain sheriffs’ officers had
selected that very time to levy on the goods and
chattels in his flat. It was not known how this
circumstance would affect his election, but in the
Carlton it is hardly considered a qualification to
entertain bailiffs.
# * *
A certain Australian medico booked his
passage by a recent mail-steamer for a run
“Home.” Between him and a prominent com-
mission-agent in the same town were contra-
accounts— the agent owing the medico .£9O, and
having a set off of £OO ; balance in favour of the
doctor, £3O. They met by appointment and
arranged that the agent’s indebtedness should
stand over until the doctor’s return from England,
some two years hence. Just here is where the
fun comes in. As the steamer was leaving
Adelaide the medico was arrested by warrant, at
the instance of the commission-agent, for a debt
of £6O, and intending to leave the colonies, &c.,
&c. Needless to say, rather than lose his passage,
the doctor at once paid up, expenses, &c., added ;
but there are sultry, times ahead for that agent.

* • •
Thp Lancet urges that the old, dirty
practice of “kissing the book” should be abolished
on account of germs and other beastly horrors,
and that witnesses should take the oath “ with the
right hand uplifted.” Why not take it with the
right foot uplifted, or on all fours if it comes
to that? Or, better still, why not abolish
the oath altogether? Like capital punishment
it really makes no difference.
* * *
A hair-brush, five Irish linen towels,
and a night-shirt, said to have been used by Wales
on the occasion of his tour through the United
States many years ago, and hoarded up by an
ardent admirer ever since, were recently sold at
New York, and realised satisfactory prices. Now,
if the proceeds of the shirt and the hair-brush and
the towels were employed as the nucleus of a fund
to console Princess May for the loss of Wales’ son,
this episode of grovel and good taste would be
about complete.

The bankruptcy examination of a Hobart
gaol official, lately dismissed for appropriating
prisoners’ valuables, threw some light on the
laches. An obliging Russian Jew, ho said, lent
him .£32 at a stiff rate and then demanded sky-
high interest under threat of exposure. One other
loan of ,£2O cost him £5 per month interest. A
church dignitary who gives a tenth of his income
to the Lord, supplied him with .£‘32 at shixty
per shent. And so on.
# # *
One of the vogues in N.S.W. bush town-
ships is for several “influential” residents to
agitate secretly for the removal of the police-
officer-in-charge, and, when successful, to get up
a testimonial to him and give him a lickspittle-
send-off.
# * *
The case, Simpson v. North Sydney Land
and Tramway Co., heard in N.S.W. Equity Court
last week, was simply the preliminary proceedings
in a big action expected to be sensational, which
the English bondholders of the Co. named intend
bringing against several prominent Melbourne and
Sydney business men, to upset the debentures
floated in London by Mr. Billyard-Leake, who
has just been declared bankrupt.
Rev. Alexander Osborne has been enter-
tained at a farewell breakfast at the Australia
Hotel, Sydney, by his fellow-clerics. The saints
all know where to get something good to eat, and,
better still, they have always got the cash to
buy it.
# # »
Only 3.96 per cent of the total number
(39,507) of N.S.W. children born last year were
vaccinated. N.S.W. is the land of quackery.
She has utterly neglected domestic legislation in
order that a humbug like Parkes might be
glorified. It is as certain that vaccination pre-
vents and modifies smallpox as that Pasteurian
inoculation prevents anthrax.
* * *
A modern writer has just concluded a
glowing address to female maturity with the
statement that “luxuriant nature, in the infinite
plentitude of her blessings, has bequeathed to
man naught else so intoxicating and incomparable
as the woman of forty.” And ho knows Venus
was forty when she first popped through the foam,
and she never grew any older.
A tram-conductor’s complaint:—•
The hair of all tram-conductors turns grey at aw early
age, because their daily traffic brings them int<? constant
contact with women, who render their lives a burden and
a terror to them.
* # *
A well-known figure on the Promenade du
Anglais at Nice (writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent)- is “Prince” Kummerer, who made his
money in wool at Sydney, and whose handsome
face used in days gone by to be so much admired
on the Block.
# # #
Christine Nilsson (writes a Bulletin
correspondent) is at Monte Carlo. ' She still
retains her good looks, her complexion being
specially admired. Her voice, alas ! has all but
left her. She thinks very highly of Melba, who
has been playing at Nice, where she had a
splendid reception malyre (or perhaps in conse-
quence of) the scandal. The Australian pnma-
donna lias grown coarse-looking and bids fair to
become a tolerably ugly middle-aged woman.
Her singing, however, is finer than ever.
The wife-killer, Williams, is booked for a rope.
But here’s a reflection that tickles—
How Justice, stern lady, will turn up her nose
When they give her cold meat without pickles.
A GHASTLY HALLAD.
The tear stands gently in my eye.
I’m almost broken-hearted ;
No need to tell the reason why—
It is the “ dear departed.”
I’m sobered hy a grief profound.
All muffled is my wrath’s tone—
My love lies in the cruel ground,
She’s underneath the hearthstone.
Sing hey! the late-lamented,
I shall never see her more :
She but recently consented
To permit me to adore ;
And a patent I’ve invented
For the lady gone before—
She is decently cemented,
Though you smell her through the floor.
’Tis very sad, but such is life !
My woe I ought to smother,
And having lost a little wife
I mean to get another—
Another young and tender spouse
To call me lord and master —
So I’ve engaged an empty house
And ordered in the plaster.
Sing hey ! my name is Deeming,
And the women like my face.
The livelong day I’m scheming
To reduce the human race ;
Of a girl whose eyes are beaming
There will shortly be no trace —
1 shall leave her sweetly dreaming
Near the good old brick fireplace.
FAIR FORTY.
Yes, give us the woman of forty,
Whose waist is sufficiently neat—
Provided her face isn’t warty,
And she hasn’t gone wrong in the feet.
Then, the wealth of her amorous knowledge
Makes her better worth winning, by far,
Than the pink and white heiress from college,
Who routs when you smoke a cigar.
The woman at forty is pleasant,
And warm as a soft summer’s eve ;
With the skill of the past and the present
Her mesh she will placidly weave.
You may talk about girls being “clever ”
And all that a man can adore—
They have not the least show whatsoever
With the charmers of twenty years more.
Then give us the woman of forty,
Who is up to each dodge in the game;
There is something reposefully naughty
In the glance of the sweet, mellow dame'.-
■ When young they are fickle and flirty.
Either laughing, or feigning a rage ;
They are wiser and better at thirty—
But forty’s the dangerous age.

In 1887, Sir M. H. Davies gave £lO,OOO to
the Melbourne charities. Vhere ish dot barty
now ? But, in the booming time, G. W. Taylor—
not the Land Creditor—promised £50,000 to the
Congregational denomination, in live annual
instalments of £lO,OOO each. They must have got
the whole of it by this time, and yet they would
rather have had it in one act.
Sir Julius Vogel is a member of the Tory
St. Stephen’s Club, London, where he plays whist
every day. He is such a confirmed cripple now
that he has to be carried up stairs. St. Stephen’s
numbers many quondam Australians amongst its
members. Ranny Want is on the committee, and
most of the Agents-General are members.
* * »
Many people after overcoming the dis-
inclination to make a will, worry for the rest of
their lives lest the lawyer who drew it, or the
nurse in their last illness, or the undertaker,
should steal it and make a fortune by selling it to
someone interested in its suppression. In S.A.
a law passed last year enables one to deposit his
will in the strong-room of the Supreme Court and
to get a receipt for it, but, up to date, only one
testator has taken advantage of the system. One
defect of the Act is that a lawyer is necessary as
a witness to the signature of the depositor of the
will—and that means a guinea.

Gov. Kintore, of S.A., declined to attend
the annual banquet of the S.A. railway engineers,
firemen, etc.—although he comes out as an expert
engine-driver himself !—on the plea that the
banqueting-night was Ash Wednesday.
* * #
The proceedings at the trial of the great
suits brought by the Queensland Investment Co.
against their directors are dismally monotonous.
E. R. Drury affects an air of supreme indifference.
He employs most of his time in smelling his
button hole, and languidly scrutinising the gallery.
F. H. Hart puffs and blows like a veritable
grampus. Sir Arthur Palmer confers anxiously
at times with his burly neighbour, theMTlwraith,
and is evidently the most uneasy of the four. Sir
Thomas, in the witness-box, was extremely like
some huge mastiff rather frightened of the attack-
ing terrier (E. M. Lilley), but endeavouring to
scare his antagonist by an assumption of gruff
dignity. One of the four jurymen seems —er— not
to be a prohibitionist.
# * #
Two warders at Kew (Melb.) Asylum
have had their wages reduced. They broke
several ribs belonging to a lunatic patient. One
warder was “docked” £ 1 per month, and the
other £2 per month. The latter broke the greater
number of ribs, we presume.
* * *
When the R.M.S. Arcadia was in the Red
Sea one Paymaster Tuck, R.N., jumped over-
board and was drowned. “ The temporary gloom
cast over the vessel was agreeably dispelled in the
evening ” (explains Melbourne Standard) “when
a first-class musical and dramatic entertainment
was provided, and games incidental to life on a
steamer indulged in.” The fact is, passengers
can’t nurse a sorrow in the Red Sea. It’s too
dashed hot. Which reminds us that some of
those “ incidental games ” (we speak of the
average mail-steamer) are pretty warm. They
mostly consist of two girls taking it in turns to be
hugged by one of the “officers” in the darkness,
and the girl who says “Don’t!” first has to pay
forfeit. Poor Tuck couldn’t expect ’em to stop
playing at that for one night, simply because lie
was dead.
* * •
“Bu rke’s Colonial Gentry” is not a reliable
authority on Australian divorces. The recent
divorce of an ex-Minister of the Crown is unnoted,
though in another case, where the wife was the
petitioner, the dissolution of the social contract is
announced, possibly for tbe information of any
gallant who is a candidate for her favour.
The site of the new Sydney Hospital was,
wo are told, originally a Crown grant free of all
restrictions, to Mrs. John Blaxland, of Newing-
ton. on her arrival in 1807. In 1812, during her
husband’s absence in England as a witness on the
trial of Capt. Johnston, Mrs. Macquarie, seeing
the necessity there was for a general hospital, fixed
her mind on this spot, and by her appeals to the
benevolence of Mrs. Blaxland induced her to sur-
render it, at the same time offering her from the
Governor the “whole of Miller’s Point” as an
equivalent, or any other land she might choose, if
at the Crown’s disposal. She left the choice to
Mr. Blaxland on his return. He, however, had
many obstacles thrown in his way, and finally
accepted .£4OO for the splendid site on which he
intended building himself a house.

* * #
A warning to fat women :
A stout woman should not wear a tailor-made suit
fitting her figure closely; it brings out every pound of
tlesh for the benefit of lookers-on. She should not wear a
rosette at her belt, either at the back or front; it makes
her look thicker through. She should not wear a lace or
ribbon ruff about her neck, though a soft feather one is
permissible, if it has long ends. She should not wear a
short skirt; it gives her a queer, dumpy look, that is
especially undesirable. She should not wear her hair low
on (he neck ; it should he high, and arranged with great
smoothness, though it need not look oily. She should
not wear beads about her neck, nor many rings on her
fingers.
# » #
A Brooklyn (U.S.) reporter lias just been
gaoled for bigamy. He was a married man and
hard-up, so he endeavoured to improve his for-
tunes by mashing an heiress named Laura Thom.
When the wedding-day came, the reporter turned
up at the altar an hour and a half late, but he
soothed the awful rage of old man Thorn by a
variety of reasons and explanations, and the cere-
mony proceeded. The joyful pair went to live at
a fashionable hotel, but at 1 a.m. the bridegroom
mysteriously cleared out, taking the wedding-ring
with him. He mysteriously turned up again about
eight, and announced that he had been away on
urgent business. When the explosion came it trans-
pired that he had made one ring do duty for both
women. The cause of the delay on the wedding
morning was that the journalist had only one
shirt, and he sat at home eating “ an oyster fry ”
while his first wife ironed that solitary garment
so that he might go out in it and marry his second
wife. When he quitted the second wife at 1 a.m.
he rushed off to spend a few hours with his first
wife lest she should grow suspicious, and then he
fled back at eight to spend a little time with the
second wife for fear she shou’d realise the situa-
tion. During his brief but tempestuous career as
a bigamist he ran back and forward between the
two wives till his legs gave out, and he was
almost glad to be captured for the sake of getting
a rest.

At Hobart’s most fashionable church the
organist and the verger lately had a gory set-to
within the sacred precincts, surrounded by a howl-
ing crowd of choristers. The affair was enlivened
by horrid shrieks of “ Murder ! Police !” from the?
aged mother of one of the combatants.

Isn’t it possible for the Australian colonies
to adopt a protective policy without the present
custom of outraging decency by engaging revenue
officers to wave women’s underlinen in the air each
time anyone travels by rail from one province to
another ?
* * *
A S.A. squatter has just died—which,
under the circumstances, was only right—but he
took a long time in going, and induced the nurse
(at 15s. per week) to attend to him day and night
by telling her every day that he had left her a nice
little sum in his will. He left many thousands,
but not a penny to that nurse.
# # #
A queer social development is the outcome
of the attitude of the R.C. Church towards the
divorce-law. You hear the R.C. members of the
Australian-Legislatures say that Roman Catholics
“ will have nothing to do with divorce.” . Yet
they do all the same—pleading afterwards, in two
cases, both of injured ladies, just brought under
The Bulletin’s notice, that “ though divorced by
the law, they are married in the sight of the
Church.” What will this sort of thing lead to?
Any man of the world can easily predict the
result, after considering that it will probably be a
less (because less sensational) offence for a legally-
divorced-but-Church-married man or woman to
indulge in contraband love than to boldly get
married again. The Tatter offence, of course, as it
amounts to openly flouting the Church and consti-
tutes, so to speak, a contempt of Church, carries
with it absolute excommunication; the former

tistry case in Melbourne the other day, that a

Fortunately, Nature has made every allowance
for this shrinkage—in Melbourne.
# # m
As a certain Sydney citizen was wending
his way home one rainy night he was accosted by
a woman in a nurse’s garb, apparently coming
from the hospital. She calmly asked for a share
of iiis umbrella. He complied. Reaching the
lady’s home, he was asked to come in and receive
mother’s thanks. Of course, he went in—of
course. Mother appeared in the form of a burly
ruffian, who demanded blackmail. The bully,
however, was sent out with a La Blanche swing
on the jaw, and the civil servant fled the shades of
Sin. Next morning, however, the bully came
round to the office of the C.S., charged him with
stealing jewellery from the helpless girl, and
wanted <£so not to lay an information. In the
X>ockets of his coat the appalled Government man
found the trinkets. He handed them over to the
bully, and told him to do his worst. The way of
the strange woman passeth all understanding.

# # #
The enterprising female is the terror of
modern society. The latest ruse of the Sydney
demi-monde is to get the passenger-list of a newly-
arrived mail-steamer, and send invitations, pur-
porting to come from some of the lady-passengers
to the officers. The gallantry of sea-faring men is
proverbial.
# # #
In Melbourne, these times, there is scarcely
a family likeness between the smiling, sympa-
thetic law for the well-backed favourite, and the
scowling, contemptuous law for the rough out-
sider. At Hawthorn Police Court, the other day,
the obsequious beaks sat with closed doors to hear
a case in which a lady, blessed with “earridge”
relatives, sued her husband for maintenance. An
arrangement to chuck out the impertinent news-
paper reporters was found to be impracticable, so
the case duly came to light, and disclosed no
colour of excuse for the magisterial conspiracy to
cover it up. Annie couldn’t get enough money
out of her husband to buy boots with, whilst that
lofty importer seemed to be addicted to stopping
out all night at the club, haw ! It was simply a
well-dressed sample of the ordinary matrimonial
row, with a strong conflict of evidence as to the
exact number of times that the importer had called
his missis “a liar.” In the end the case was
dismissed, whereupon the closed doors were
thrown open, and complainant rustled off again
in the blessed family coach. There is no moral to
this story, except that the Hawthorn magistrates
haven’t been requested to resign.
# # #
A blackfellow recently charged at Narrabri
(N.S.W.) with putting a log on the railway line,
explained that he wanted to see if the train would
jump it. He knew it was fast on the flat but
wished to find out if it was a steeplechaser.

ENGLISH, YOU KNOW!
The season in Sydney is dawning again,
So maidens, in quest of a bean,
Are curling their lips with a “ tongey ” disdain,
And putting on airs from over the main—
For ’lis O ! to be English, you know.
They’ll mourn for the Duke at the forthcoming ball,
And sport a black ribbon of woe ;
The names of the nobles who carried the pall
They will probably lisp with a thoroughbred drawl—
That’s awfully English, you know.
The fashion has spread, since the cricketers came,
Of hating “colonial blow,”
And the new-season angel will “ think it a shame
That Australia played such a wearisome game ”
And beat the poor English, you know.
The dresses, my dear ! well, we mustn’t collapse
If we find them exceedingly low,
Nor blush to discover young Phyllis perhaps
Reduced to a skirt and a couple of straps
The latest in English, you know.
All fellers from “ home ” are delicious this year,
Our natives will not have a show ;
/ mean to get ready my bow and my spear
And capture the Johnnie who ventures too near,
For ’tis O!to be English, you know.
J.P.S.
THE SONG OF THE STAMPERS.
Again I hear them throbbing with a murmur like the sea,
When afar she holds her siege upon the shore,
The stampers are repeating low their melody to me,
The lullaby they sang me on the nights that are no
more.
1 doze before, the tent again, beneath the brooding gums,
The forty head are going at the Pilot ’cross the creek ;
There’s method in their music like the roll of martial
drums,
And the voices of old comrades through their mellow
measure speak.
The ghosts of voices long since dumb the battery’s mean-
ings bring
From out the grave of memory, and dreamily I hear
A tone that was forgotten and a song we never sing
Since Duncan missed the ladders in the fatal British
Peer.
Now they swell the solemn strophe. In the rhythm that
they beat
Upon the quartz another and a stranger meaning
seems—
’Tis the passing of an army with a myriad mailed feet,
Marching on for ever through the fairy realm of dreams.
It is softer now, sublimer, like the music of the spheres,
The organ tones seraphic from Immensity that roll,
To murmur inspiration in adoring poets’ ears,
Or woo the dainty Queen of Sleep and wed her to a
soul.
Now silence, harsh, oppressive, and a sense of something
wrong.
I start awake and listen, with a feeling ’kin to fright;
The boys are using language that is secular and strong.
The battery is “ hung up ” and they’ll sleep no more
to-night.
E. D.

THE BEALS PATENT
HIGH ARM SEWING MACHINES
Have, by their continued merits and the general
satisfaction they have invariably given, won the
first place in public esteem, and are on all sides
admitted to be the best and most useful sewing
machines in the market.
FOR tJSE IN THE HOUSEHOLD
The Beale Machine presents innumerable advant-
ages. The mechanism and the adjustments are
perfect, while the working is most simple) and
the addition of all the most recent inventions and
improvements renders the manipulation of every-
thing connected with the machine a matter of
perfect ease. The result of this is, that with
ordinary care the machine will last for many
years, nothing can get out of order, and iio time
is Wasted in tiresome meddling with the parts.
It is a servant that will never grumble, never
rebel, never want nights out, willing even on
washing days. It only asks for a careful cleaning
how and then, a snug, dry corner alid a little
genuine sewing machine oil. Any other oil will
make it sick, and dirt of all kind it abhors.
The Self-threading* Shuttle, of solid steel,
saves the trouble of threading by the end of the
cotton. It has an excellent tension, is strong and
simple;
The Bobbix! Winder is a marvel of in-
genuity, Winding attd guiding the cotton by
itself, as evenly as upon the reel whence it came,
and stopping of its own accord when the bobbin
is fulh
Eor all kinds of family sewing and fancy the
Beale Machine has the greatest capacity.
The Improved High Arm. A great de-
sideratum with ladies has been the raising of the
arm of the machine, so as to allow the goods to be
sewn freely to pass underneath. This had to be
accomplished without increasing the weight of the
machine. In fact, the Beale runs with wonderful
ease and rapidity. It is almost silent, all clicking
sounds having been obviated. As is well known,
a good test of the finish of any mechanism is its
silence when in rapid motion.
With the Wonderful Beale Machine,
whether for hand or foot use, you can hem any
width, make double seams or fells, braid stitch
with chenille, Berlin wool, or gold cord, and such
like fancy work ; you may make a pretty lace
[ stitch, tuck ever so finely, bind dresses, coats, or
carpets ; sew on trimmings without guiding, make
frilling, ruffling, or niching; quilt, weave your
own braid with any combination of colours and
stitch it on at the same time ; make thick, woollen
door-mats, embroidered antimacassars, window
curtains, and so forth ; you can easily and rapidly
perform the fashionable shirring or gathering so
popular for sleeves, bodies, or skirts of dresses
with an appliance supplied gratis ; lay the small
cords in hoods and stays ; do large cording ; gather
with or without a band, and generally do all
kinds of straight sewing upon any material what-
ever. You can darn upon any woven goods, and
do crewel-work. The Beale Patent will pass
from eight folds of tweed or twenty of calico to
two of thinnest muslin, or even mosquito-net or
tulle, without the operator changing needle,
cotton, stitch, or tension, and the fine material
will not be gathered in the slightest. You may
make button and eyelet-holes, do over-casting,
make herring-bone stitch upon any materials.
This work is absolutely unique and can only be
< accomplished upon the Beale Patent Machine.
Any of these performances will be shown at our
shops at any time. The Beale Patent makes
a lock-stitch, which cannot ravel, yet by a momen-
tary alteration the sewing can be done so as to
rip from end to end, if This peculiarity
is sometimes useful when children’s clothes are
intended to be altered.
Nickel-plating.— Tn our climate the exposed
parts of the machine, and the applicances, need
to be protected from rust. We have, at consider-
able expense, added a handsomely nickeled wheel,
nickeled plates, screws, appliances, treadle-bar
and cones. Heightening the appearance, these
improvements also tend largely to preserve the
parts. This plating is imperishable.
A special feature of The Beale Machine is
that
ALL THE CABINET WORK

AUSTRALIAN WOODS,
some of which are without doubt the most beauti-
ful in the world. Suitability to the climate is
thus insured, and we are enabled to offer a greater
variety of styles, most handsome in design and
conveniently fitted for all the requirements of a
lady’s work-table.
These machines can be inspected at any time at

AN ALLEGORY.
fhe fight vims over; and the battle won.
A soldier—who beneath his chieftain’s eye
Had done a mighty deed and dofie it well,
Arid done it as the world will have it done—
A stab, si ctirse, some qdick play of the butt,
Two skiills cracked crosswise, but the colours
~ saved —
Proud of his vvOunds, proud of the promised cross,
Turned to his rear-rank man, who On his gun
Leant heavily apart. “Ho ! friend,” he called,
“ You did not fight then, were you left behind?
I saw you not.” The other turned and showed
A gaping, red-lipped wound upon his breast.
“ Ah,” said he sadly, “I was in the smoke.”
Threw up his arms, shivered, and fell and died.

A MAGNIFICENT ILLUMINANT.
As the long nights approach heads of families
are called upon to consider what is the
best light for the home.
The electric light is not available, and even if it
were its installation in the great majority of
houses would be something out of the question.
GAS IS TOO EXPENSIVE,
as well as being dangerous and vile smelling at
times,- A good, cheap, portable light is un-
doubtedly the light to suit the masses.
LUXENE,
the new American Illuminant, commands the
attention of all. It is without doubt the light for
the people. Luxene gives out no smell or smoke
in burning, and is absolutely
PROOF AGAINST EXPLOSION,
and may be used in an ordinary kerosene lamp.
The enormous increased demand for Luxene since
its introduction into Australasia is the best
evidence of its popularity and utility. Ask your
storekeeper for
LUXENE, THE NEW ILLUMINANT,
and insist upon his supplying it. It needs only
to be shown and used to ensure a continuance of
its use.
HE WAS GRATEFUL.
Miners as a rule, are brave men, and one of
their strong points is a sense of gratitude. One of
the class, a bronzed, weather-beaten toiler, by
name Albert Tucker, walked one afternoon into
the head agency of the Waterbury Watch Com-
pany in Sydney, and demanded to be presented
to “the boss.” His wish being gratified, he pro-
ceeded to explain that “ he was only down for the
day,” but he had determined the first time he was
in Sydney to come along and state what he knew
about “the most wonderful of watches,” The
boss had reckoned himself blush proof, but the
string of etdogiums that Mr. Tucker let off with-
out taking breath brought up a ruddy glow never
before suspected to exist. “I bought a short-
wind Waterbury for 225. fid. some two years ago
from your agent at Trunkey,” he said. “Since
then I have worn it constantly above and below
ground, and in all temperatures, and the way it
has kept time almost to the second under all con-
ditions is a positive wonder. If I did not know
where to get another Waterbury, I would not take
.£8 for this one. I thought,” he concluded by
observing, “it was only right to come and tell
you, for it’s the best watch I ever saw or heard
tell of, and everybody ought to know about it.”
The latest developments in Waterburys are the
new and improved watches for ladies and gentle-
men, known respectively as the “N” and “K
series. These are procurable in nickel, sterling
silver, or rolled gold cases (with 15 years’
guarantee), and while being inexpensive are highly
artistic in appearance, and for reliability are
second to no other timekeeper in the world.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The celebrated pugilist whose passage-money to
America was generously advanced by Frank Coffee, of
Sydney, has never written a line to his benefactor since
his departure.
# * #
“ Plunk ! The smaller the boy the bigger the
sinker.” —Proverbs of Joe Lowe, vul. 1%, pdye 108 U.
* * *
The largest attendance at any cricket-match in
the world was in Melbourne in January, during the four
d ys’ match, Australia v. England, when 75,000 people
paid. The highest one-day number was 23,000, which
was, however, beaten during the visit of Ivo Bligh’s team
in 1882. The world’s record was 30,000, at a match
played on Kensington Oval.
# # *
Lord Sheffield says that his team’s cricket-tour
has not resulted in a profit.
# a *
Strathmore’s dam, Ouida, by Yattendon —
Young Gulnare, was bought by an Auckland sport in the
very early eighties. She was a black of no particular
shape or make, and was put into the hands of Edward
Day to “train.” The mare never could gallop any-
how, hut Mr. Morriu saw how few Yattendons we had
and how good a strain it was —vide Yatterina, Chester,
Grand Flaneur, &c. —and bought her slabsided ladyship
for the stud. That he showed good judgment has been
well proved by the prices brought by her progeny, and
the rare excellence of this greatest of her sons, Strath-
more.
» # #
The death of that handsome delusion, Gatling,
grieves nobody except proprietor Gannon. The horse
never won a race and might have lived for another ten
years without improving his record. It w'as just this way
with Gatling. He might finish second in a field of ten,
takeaway the winner and run the race over again, and
Gatling would finish second to something else. So on
until each of the nine horses had beaten him. Had there
been nothing left for Gatling but to walk over the course
and take the slakes, lie would have dropped down dead
rather than pass the judge’s box.
* # »
Grace made a strange mistake in not putting
Briggs on to bowl in the Victorians’first innings, when
the wicket was dull and just suited to him. In the
second innings, although the pitch was then much more
lively, the little Lancashire lad took 5 for 33 runs. Briggs
comes from a damp soddened county, and on a wicket
that resembles his native heath he is still the most
dangerous howler the Britishers can turn on. Sharpe is
no good at all when the iron has gone out of the ground,
and will he quite a “passenger” in the Adelaide match
if there is soft weather about.
# # *
Altogether, last week’s match on the M.C.C.
ground gave a fair idea of the Britishers’ real “form.”
They won not by making a lot of runs, but because they
were able to get a number of moderate or unbrilliant
batsmen out at small expense. None of their representa-
tive sloggers played as freely and prettily as Bruce, and
the two "centurions” of the last N.S.W. match—Read
and Lehmann-were only good for three runs between
them.
# • *
An old Sydney gentleman, deaf enough to defy
Gabriel’s trumpet, was the other day fooling round a
pony, which suddenly let out with both heels and just
missed the pit of the ancient’s stomach—so small a dis-
tance that tlie old fellow felt the sharp fan of the air. To
him an onlooker—“ Obh I did he kick you very hard?”
“Kick me? ’ said the O.G. “ where?” The other pointed
t) the centre of the old fellow’s waistcoat. Whereupon
the O.G became very ill indeed, begged to be taken
inside, and sent for a doctor, w ho put him on gruel diet
and did a lot of other things until the old-’un was within
an ace of making his final will and testament. “But the
doctor pulled me through, after ail,” the old gent will
tell you ; “ my word, that Dosem is a clever fellow !”
* # #
A determined attack by a shark on a boat
occurred at Twofold, N.S.W., the other day. Th> boat, a
20-footer, had her centreboard lowered, and the occupants
were killing schnapper, when the craft was almost lifted
from the water, and looking out the fishers were horrified
to see a monster shark dart off some 20 feet from the
boat, and turning like a flash smash straight into her
again. Three times did be charge, and whilst one threw
out fish to the brute the others cut the line and got sail
on the boat, the shark following half way across the Bay,
On reaching shore the boat was hauled up, when it was
seen that the centreboard was bent, both garhoard
streaks cracked, and pieces cut out of the hardwood keel
by the shark’s teeth. The occupants of the boat surmise
that the centreboard being painted red attracted the
shark, and when he rose at it, the depth of the board
prevented his taking a bigger mouthful. Had the centre-
hoard been a few inches less in depth he must have torn
the garhoard streaks out.

A curious figure in Melbourne is the blind
brother of “ Mo.” Trahan, the well-known jock. He
walks about the city unattended, and, unless you look
closely at him, you would never imagine he was blind'.
He always walks very fast, and is generally sworn at for
running into people, who do not know his condition. He
is a regular attendant at race meetings, and bets pretty
heat ily.

The other day a very ricli Australian racing man
nominated a horse for a race, at Randwick. Ten servants
in a Sydney iiotel clubbed- together anti bought a sweep-
ticket and drew the horse referred to. One of them
approached the magnate and'asked him if they were going
to have a good run. “ Yes, ’he replied, “if you lay me
£400.” And they laid him the £4OO, did those servants,
and the rich man actually accepted that wager. It hap-
pened that the hors? didn’t start.

The Souther training-farm, California, possesses
the novelty of a salt-water swimming-tank, 90ft. long,.
20ft, wide, and Bft. deep. A swinging platform, is sust
pended from the roof, and' from this a man guides the
swimming horse. Plenty work can he given the horse,
and the benefit claimed for the tank is summed up in>
three words —“ work without concussion.”

When Joe Thompson, Australia’s representative
bookmaker, was on his way to England, a certain youth
at the cabin-table used to outvie Joe in drawing the long
•how, whereat the Leviathan felt much aggrieved. On the
young man one day telling an extra-strong yarn, Thomp-
son chipped in—“ And you really saw the thing happen
yourself?” “I did.” replied the champion liar. “Well,”

all right; but, s’help me hob, if I’d seen it myself I
wouldn’t have believed it.”

Some wonderful hitting was shown on Saturday
-at the Adelaide Oval, in the match South Adelaide v.
Norwoods. George Giffen twice knocked the ball clean
out of the ground for six. Lyons hit a fiver, and Walter
Giffen struck two fivers and one six. Thirty-three for
six hits takes a lot of beating.
# # #
One of the “ clever ’ crowd nearly got at one of
the big “ books ” pretty extensively, not many weeks ago.
The owner of a well-known horse strolled into Sydney
Tattersall’s, and entering into conversation with the book-
maker in question, informed him that his had no
show in a certain race, whereupon the “book,” in the
language of his craft, asked if he might consider the
horse “in the hag.” Receiving a favourable reply, he
commenced to pepper the nag, and laid £lOOO to £3O five
times over; it afterwards transpired the wagers were
taken in the interests of the owner. The horse did not
win, hut it was not the fault of the owner, who sent him
for all he was worth.
# # *
Some time since The Bulletin told of a pony-
owner who was “ badly left ” by his jockey, who mistook
the “office” given him, and did not “try,” although the
stable had a pot of money on the prad. Well, the same
owner started a pony at a suburban meeting, in a selling-
race, a few weeks ago, there being three started, No. 1,
owned by the unfortunate under notice ; No. 2, owned by
another clever crowd ; and No. 3, a rank outsider without
a 1000 to 1 stiow. The No. 1 division “stiffened” their
representative and hacked No. 2, whilst the No. 2 crowd
also did a hit of clever business by backing No. 1 and
“stiffening” No. 2. When No. 3, a veritable cripple,
won, the language used by both the clever divisions was
positively awful.
# * *
If George Hill means business with Marvel for
the Sydney Cup, he should shake hands with himself, for,
in the writer’s opinion, Marvel, with 9st. 91b., is the
easiest weighted horse in the race. A good many people
believe Marvel cannot race over a mile, but a horse that
has won the Waverley Handicap carrying lOst. 51b.-a
mile and three-quarters race—in 3inin. 6sec., has shown
himself to be something more than a miler. There is good
authority for stating that Marvel was never so well in his
life as at time of writing.

Hellings, the sixteen year old youngster who
recently won the 200 yards N.S.W. amateur swimming
contest, has the pluck essential to a great athlete, llel-
lings, until quite lately, used to steer crews from the Wool-
loomooloo Rowing Sheds. His home was across the Bay,
and it was a common evening incident for big Kennedy
to pose upon the balcony of the Sydney shed and signal
for Hellings who would respond by embarking upon a
plank and ferrying with one scull across the water.
Deibler, the Parisian executioner, aged
60, is retiring. The list of his victims total 218.
Under the proposed Shiels’ reductions of
salaries, Victorian Cabinet Ministers will now
only receive from <£1295 to £1850 per annum
instead of from £1400 to £2000 a year. Members
of Parliament will get £277 10s. instead of £300.
“The second (the extra vote for “Thrift”)
proposal had to be sent back into the womb of
Conservatism to be re-born, and Mr. Service was
asked by the Argus to become a midwife to bring
Mr. L. L. Smith’s monstrosity into existence and
to shape its features. The Argus complained
that Mr. Service’s plan was both intelligible
and complete ; but the Argus plan was both un-
intelligible and incomplete. The public had to
read the Service mystery by the light of the
Argus botch.” —Premier Shiels, Vie.
Premier Shiels, of Victoria, has announced
his policy. Briefly stated, it consists of equalising
the finances this year, amending the land tax in
relation to “unimproved values ” next year, and
submitting a One-man-one-vote Bill the year
after. To equalise the finances Shiels proposes to
reduce salaries and expenses, and to increase
revenue in various ways. The salaries of Civil
Servants will be thus affected Non clerical up
to £120, clerical up to £150, and professional up
to £170 —not to be touched ; all salaries between
these sums and £300— 2h per cent reductions ;
all over £300— 7h per cent reductions. Only the
Governor’s and the Supreme Court Judges’
salaries are to be exempt. Train mileage is to be
reduced and various railway economies exercised.
The “tuppeny” country post is to be reverted
to; the stock-tax increased ; and land imposts
are to be levied on the unimproved capital value
of the soil. An income-tax is proposed on all in-
comes above £300 a year, differentiating between
incomes derived from land, or property, and
those acquired by industry or professional exer-
tion. Customs duties are to be increased on line.s
as yet necessarily unindicated. Notwithstanding
all monetary difficulties, Shiels hopes to spend 1|
millions sterling on railways during the next 18
months. He also promises : A Trades Disputes
Bill; a Bill to Abolish Bogus Banks and audits ;
the purchase of freehold lands with inscribed stock,
and the settlement thereon in small farms of time-
payment purchasers; a Government Labour
Bureau; continued butter bonuses ; and the re-
valuation of water-supply schemes.

This year’s French classe of recruits—the
twenty-first since the war—is the smallest known
s nee the new law came into force. This is due to
the enormous mortality among children caused by
the wide-spread distress of 1370-71, and, in Paris,
by the horrors of the siege.
A Melbourne weekly, having arisen from
the ruins of another paper which collapsed under
a very similar name, recommends itself to notice
in a painfully original way. The same old staff,
runs the announcement, will continue to speak
with the same old voice, in the same old words
that couldn’t find a satisfactory hearing in the
first instance.
There is a hard case up North named
Paddy M . One of the men in his employ, tired
of perpetual (suetless) salt-horse and water dough-
boys, said to him, “ I wish the missus would put
a bit of suet or fat in the dough-boys.” “ Listen
to that now, listen to that, Bridget,” exclaimed
Paddy, “its pasthry the blayguards are after
wanting.”
Providence recently sent a large numbe r
of disabled fish for the relief of the Melbourne
unemployed. It is supposed that the factories
around Footscray had been poisoning the Yarra
more than usual, and as the mullet, eels, &c. came
floating down atop of the stream, groaning in
th°ir agony, the inhabitants of Oollingwood
district netted the slippery sufferers in their hats
and umbrellas, and other household utensils, and
Mib'equently ate them “without feeling any ill
effects. ”
An American lady is Bow dierising the poet
Burns—eliminating from his verses all coarseness
and allusions to alcohol. As well rob professional
teetotal-apostles of their cold water. What
would they bo without cold-water ? Merely milk.
Fairplay (London) says that in Jive weeks
49 captains have died of yellow fever at Santos.
One ship sent ten hands to hospital, of whom
seven died. Another ship lost three captains in
three weeks. The chief cause of the fever is local
filth.
Williams, the woman-hacker, presented
his latest Bathurst (N.S.W.) fiancee with “ his
murdered wife’s rings,” and also wanted to give
the girl the dead woman’s clothes. The sort of
man who, while he keeps out of gaol, gets an extra
vote for “ Thrift.”
The Duke of Westminster, recently
enriched a London charity with £500 at one fell
swoop. The ducal “monkey” represented ten
thousand shillings paid by a like number of sight-
seers for admission to view the interior of his
ancestral home, Eaton Hall. This is a nice, easy
way of raising the wind for benevolence, but
Westminster might, by a little self-sacrifice,
double the amount of his alms-giving. He ought
to charge “two bob a nob” and include himself
in the spectacle. Everyone would pay a shilling
extra to see a duke worth a million a year silting
in state,

Some Sydney speculators have just secured
a lease from the Queensland Government of a
guano island in the Gulf of Carpentaria for .£30 a
year, which island is estimated to contain 4,000,000
tons of guano.
“ The clergyman is selected for his office
while he is very young, and long before he has
had time or lias acquired sufficient intellectual
expansion to bo able to comprehend the nature
and scope of the great subject to which his life is
to be devoted. His mind is carefully trained to
believe the tenets of a particular church, to
defend and to teach them and them alone, and to
carry on ceaseless war against the opposing tenets
of other churches; and the fulfilment of these
narrow functions during the whole of his pro-
fessional life is attempted to be enforced by
sanctions highly penal in their personal, social,
and professional consequences. How can a mind
so trained, and harshly compelled to submit to
such discipline, exercise the commanding power of
a real teacher over the intellect, differently con-
stituted, ever otherwise occupied, and constantly
subject to influences so wholly diverse, of the
educated, thinking layman at this day? The
thoughts of the two men are not in unison ; there
is no intellectual sympathy, no common intellec-
tual interest between them in regard to a large
number of the topics and arguments which the
clergyman is constrained to select for his pulpit
utterances. ’’—George Higinbotham,

Dear Bulletin, — Re “T.’s” letter anent
non inquesting of body near Dandaloo or Gongol-
gon, we have not to go to such a back-bloeky
country for similar treatment of bush corpses.
Last year a party of revellers left Green’s gunyah,
one on horseback and several in a waggonette.
The horseman perished, and was found a few
miles off in a drawn-up position, but not drowned,
only dead. Two of the others got out of the
waggonette close to Brookong station and died—
not from drowning, as reported. Another, who was
in the vehicle was, with difficulty and whisky,
kept alive after being deposited at the station.
There was no inquest on any of the three. I have
written to the Minister for Justice and Mr.
Arthur Rae, M.L.A., pointing out that in a case
of mineral poisoning it is not even now too late to
call upon the analyst. Anyway, I want to know
how my special coroner is paid. I know he is
tremendously oppressed by the work of one-man-
several billets, because he has not time to answer
even business letters. —Yours, Hikam.
